JOHN CAMPBELL, BANKER

Duncan Campbell, Knight of Loch Awe, who became Lord Campbell in 1445, married (a) Marjory, daughter of the Duke of Albany, son of Robert II, and (b) Margaret, daughter of Sir John Stewart, a son of Robert III. By the first marriage the knight had a son, Archibald, who was the founder of the House of Argyll; Archibald’s son, Colin, became first Earl of Argyll in 1457. By his second marriage Sir Duncan Campbell had a son, Colin, who was the first laird of Glenorchy, ancestor of the Campbells of Glenorchy and Breadalbane. Both the Argyll and the Breadalbane families could claim royal descent.

In lines 817–25 we should expect to find reference to contemporary chiefs, but the Earl of Breadalbane mentioned here is the first Earl, who died in 1717, and we cannot identify the others without a brief account of the Dukes of Argyll and Earls of Loudon.

The tenth Earl of Argyll, an active supporter of the Prince of Orange, was created a duke in 1701. On his death in 1703 he was succeeded by his son John (1680–1743), who had a notable military record in Marlborough’s campaigns, attaining the rank of Lieutenant-General. He was in command of the forces in Spain in 1710 and in Scotland in 1712; he fought against Mar at Sheriffmuir in 1715 and suppressed the first Jacobite rebellion. After a chequered political career, the second Duke retired into private life and died in 1743, being succeeded by his brother, Archibald.

The third Duke (1682–1761) was Treasurer of Scotland in 1705 and a strong supporter of the Union; he played a leading part in Scottish affairs and was distinguished in letters as well as in politics. He and Duncan Forbes of Culloden were the first to

[TD 439]

propose the raising of Highland regiments. From him the dukedom passed to his cousin, John Campbell of Mamore.

The fourth Duke (1693–1770) had a long military career. He was aide-de-camp to his chief in 1715, and he fought at Dettingen in 1744. In the “Forty-five” he was in command of the Government forces in the west of Scotland, while his son John (b. 1723) was in command of the Argyll militia. (See note on Battle of Falkirk.)

It would appear that the poet was thinking of the second Duke, but the description would fit the fourth Duke also, in which case the date of the poem would fall between 1761 and 1768.

Lord Loudon was descended from the Campbells of Glenorchy. Colin, the first laird of Glenorchy, had two sons—Duncan, who succeeded as second laird, and John, who became first laird of Lawers. The Campbells of Lawers were thus the senior cadet family of the Campbells of Glenorchy. In 1633 John Campbell, eldest son of the fourth laird of Lawers, married Margaret, Baroness of Loudon in her own right. This John Campbell, first Earl of Loudon, was High Chancellor of Scotland in 1641; he was a strong supporter of the Covenant and was an officer in the Scottish army at the battle of Newburn.

Later, Lord Loudon was present at the coronation of Charles II at Scone and fought at Dunbar; he supported the Glencairn rising but soon submitted to Monk.

The first Earl died in 1663 and was succeeded by his son James, who had no sympathy with the measures of government adopted by Charles II. He went abroad and died in Leyden in 1684.

Hugh, the third Earl of Loudon, was a representative peer to the first parliament of Great Britain, fought at Sheriffmuir against Mar, and was High Commissioner to the Church of Scotland. He died in 1731, and his son, John (1705–82), succeeded.

The fourth Earl of Loudon was aide-de-camp to George II in 1743 and raised Loudon’s regiment in 1745. Appointed Adjutant-General to Sir John Cope, he lost his regiment at Prestonpans and was sent north to raise a force for the Government. He relieved the garrison at Fort Augustus, made a feeble attempt to capture the Prince at Moy Hall, and fled from Inverness into Sutherland, whence he embarked with 800 men for Skye. He occupied Lochaber after Culloden.

Lord Loudon was appointed General in command in Virginia and of the British forces in America in 1756, but was recalled

[TD 440]

owing to popular clamour against his inactivity when war was declared against France. He was sent to assist the Portuguese against the Spaniards, and afterwards was appointed Governor of Edinburgh Castle.

Evidently the poet was referring to the fourth Earl, though he cannot be described as a victorious commander.

The subject of this poem, John Campbell, was, according to tradition, the son of Colin of Ardmaddie, son of the first Earl of Breadalbane by his second marriage. Colin’s mother was a daughter of the ninth Earl of Argyll, so that he was connected with the two great Campbell families. About 1692 the Earl of Breadalbane bought the castle and lands of Ardmaddie in Netherlorn from Lord Neil Campbell, second son of the eighth Earl of Argyll. Colin Campbell of Ardmaddie died in London in 1708.

The future banker was brought up at Finlarig Castle, near Killin, received a good education, and in due course became a writer in Edinburgh. He was appointed assistant secretary to the Royal Bank in 1732, second cashier in 1734, and principal cashier in 1745, in time to have official dealings with Murray of Broughton during the occupation of Edinburgh by the Jacobite army.

John Campbell “of the Bank” was an important personage in his day, a man of ability and culture, of kindly manner and handsome presence. He died in 1777.

His daughter, Mary, married Alexander Campbell, seventh laird of Barcaldine, in 1785. Their daughter, Helen Maria, married in 1818 the minister of Ardchattan, Rev. Hugh Fraser; the eldest son of this marriage was Professor Campbell Fraser of Edinburgh University, who died in 1914. (See Famed Breadalbane, pp. 71, 175; Book of Barcaldine, pp. 109, 118; F, p. 492.)

In Sàr Obair, p. 238, there is a story that the banker received the poem very ungraciously and refused to pay the expected fee on the ground that the ode was but a record of fact, and that in any case the authorship was in doubt. Challenged to compose a song of dispraise, the poet (according to Sàr Obair) began “in the same measure,” and this is the stanza quoted in F, p. 494*:

<gai>An oidhche bha mi an Tigh na Sràid†
Fhuair mi tàmailt a bha mór,
Fhuair mi stràc de ’n bhata chaol
Bho Iain claon an Achadh Mhóir.‡<eng>


* But see quotation from Records of Argyll in the Introduction, p. xxvi.

† Killin Hotel.

‡ The house of Auchmore, near Killin.

[TD 441]

This verse is not typical of Donnchadh Bàn and is certainly not “in the same measure.” More relevant as a parody is the verse quoted by Mr John Munro at a Donnchadh Bàn anniversary céilidh held in Oban in March 1904:

<gai>Iain Caimbeul a’ bhanca
Gum faicinn thu slàn,
Le d’ churran ’s le d’ chàl
’S le d’ bhuntàta gun fheòil;
’S tu marcach an t-seann eich
As bramanta ceum,
Le d’ shrathair ’s le d’ chléibh,
A’ toirt dhachaigh na mòin’;
Maide crom air a dhroll …<eng>

This extempore composition might well induce the banker to pay the bard’s fee of half a crown.

It is, however, difficult to believe that the bard’s tribute would be treated so contemptuously or that the poet would show disrespect to one for whom he feels such admiration and esteem as the poem reveals. This song is, indeed, one of Macintyre’s happiest compositions, a bardic tribute of neatly turned and elaborately constructed stanzas, dedicated to a worthy man.

METRE

The stanza falls into four parts—P, Q, R and S, each having four lines. In most stanzas the first two lines of R may be read as three double-stressed phrases completed by a dactyl, and the second two lines similarly; in other stanzas R may be arranged as two long lines having internal rhyme and dactylic end-rhyme. P, Q and S are similar to each other in construction: second and third lines have end-rhyme, and in most cases the first stressed syllables of the third and fourth lines correspond. If the rhyme sequence is indicated by abbc, the a-rhyme is repeated initially in the second line, the b-rhyme of P is repeated in Q but not in S, and the c-rhyme is on ò throughout the poem.

TUNE

Gesto Coll., p. 137; another set in Orain a’ Mhòid, XVI, p. 14.

Both forms cuilbheir and cuilìobhair were used, as were “caliver” and “caliever” in English. This was an early (seventeenth-century) gun, lighter and shorter than the musket. Cf. crowd, Welsh crwth, an old six-stringed violin; four of the strings were played upon by a bow, while the other two were plucked or played as an accompaniment. Cf. Dorothy Brown (Sàr Obair, p. 63): Mo chruit, mo chlàrsach a’s m’ fhiodhall.

813–28. The reigning Earl of Breadalbane was not the Earl that raided Caithness, and it is difficult to follow the train of thought here, unless the poet is enumerating the great Campbell houses that would welcome the banker, and naming a distinguished representative of each.

833. Both on his father’s and on his mother’s side, Colin Campbell of Ardmaddie could claim royal blood.

CAPTAIN DUNCAN CAMPBELL

Sir Robert Campbell, third baronet and ninth laird of Glenorchy, granted the lands of Auchlyne in Glendochart to his youngest son, Duncan. The first laird of Auchlyne died in 1703 and was succeeded by his son Robert, who died before 1740. The third laird, Duncan, son of Robert, had two sons, James born in 1731 and John in 1739. This James was the last laird of Auchlyne.

According to the poem, Duncan Campbell belonged to this family. In a letter to John Campbell of the Bank, Campbell of Achallader, factor to Breadalbane, says he was unable to give the Captain’s exact relationship to the lairds of Auchlyne. James MacGregor, son of Rob Roy, claims Captain Campbell as a near

[TD 444]

relation, the Captain being a nephew of MacGregor of Glengyle.

The subject of this poem was a captain in the Argyll militia in 1745; he was appointed a captain of the Edinburgh City Guard in 1751 and died in 1774 (F, p. 497).

After the battle of Culloden, Captain Campbell was in command of a company detailed to search and harass the districts of Arisaig and Moidart. He refrained from executing the harsh measures prescribed by Cumberland, and won the gratitude and esteem of the people. (See footnote in E, p. 61, repeated in F, p. 497; also Famed Breadalbane, p. 245.)

Alexander MacDonald, who had no love for the Clan Campbell, paid a remarkable tribute to the Captain in his poem An Airc. (See note, 979.)

TITLE

A, Oran do Chaiptean Donnacha Caimbeul ann Geard Dhuineidin; B, Oran do Chaiptean Geard Dhuineidin; CD, Oran do Chaiptein Geard Dhun-eidin; E, Oran do Chaiptin Geard Dhuneideann; F, as text, except Dhun-éideinn.

METRE

Oran. The stanza may be arranged in four long lines, each with four stresses and internal rhyme; the four lines have trochaic end-rhyme.

TEXT

In some cases at least, the spike had a screw thread at the base to fit into a socket in the boss of the shield. It could be readily fixed for action or removed when not in use.

962. E, sgeanamhail; F, sgeanmhail.

964. ABCDEF, an taoim. Blade and handle have been described in detail, and 964 presumably deals with the sheath or its attachments. The dirk was attached to the kilt-belt, as described in Inv. G.S. Tr., XXVI, p. 241:

Is luchd tarruinn nam biodag, / ’Bhiodh air criosan an fhéile.

Similarly Inv. G.S. Tr., XXII, p. 239:

Bheir làmh air a’ bhiodaig, / A tha air crios ’s an truaill.

The following quotation, supplied by Mr Angus Matheson, from No. 16 of Maclagan MSS., indicates the means of attachment:

<gai>O ’s ioma fear goiseid (gòirseid)
Le ghuna boidheach ’s lann du-ghorm,
Le ’n cuinnseiribh caola,
’S an taidhmin ’gan giùlan …<eng>

Here an taidhmin = an taidhmean, their “straps,” supporting the whingers. (For the forms taoim, taidhm, cf. maoim, maidhm; snaoim, snaidhm.) Thus taoim appears to be the link between the dirk-sheath and waist-belt—a link or chain, a cord or strap. Cf. also Gillies, p. 299:

<gai>Crios do leathar na’n aighin / Ceann leathann is deadh thaim air.<eng>

Mr Matheson suggests a possible connection with Eng. team,* which meant (a) part of a gear by which oxen or horses were harnessed to a plough, (b) a strong iron chain, chain in general; or development, by normal loss of final vowel, from tuidme, act of fastening, fixing, binding; tuidhme = coimhcheangal, O’Cl. (RIA Dict., Contribb.); tuidhme, binding together, union (Dinn); tuidhme, confederacy, conjunction (Dwelly); tuidhmim, I yoke, couple (Lhuyd).

971. A, ditheamh; BC, dithe; D, dìthe; E, dibhe.


* Cf. W. Fris. team, bridle; Dutch toom, bridle; etc.

[TD 446]

979. dùbailt’, referring to Macintyre’s own poem, and MacDonald’s tribute in An Airc (AMD, p. 256), from which three stanzas are quoted below.

Stanzas from MacDonald’s eulogy of Captain Campbell:

<gai>Tha e méineach iochdmhor bàidheil
Tròcaireach càirdeach, làn athtruais,
Guineach ri naimhdean ’sna blàraibh
Gus an tàradh e fo smachd iad.

An sin thaisbeanadh e tròcair
Do bhochdaibh fògarrach gach àite;
Cha chuireadh e an gnìomh an t-òrdugh
Bha bho ’n Fheòladair ’na phàtent.

Beannachd nochd is bhochd sin Mhùideirt,
Beannachd gach dùthcha ’nad chuideachd,
A bhrìgh nach do chùir thu le dòlas
Sean no òg dhiubh chum na h-uidil.<eng>

[He is tender, humane, kindly,
merciful, friendly, full of pity,
fierce against his foes in battles,
till he accomplished their subjection.

Then he would show compassion
to the outlawed poor of every district;
he would not execute the order
put by the Butcher in his warrant.

Blessing from these stripped, poor folk of Moidart,
blessing from each place goes with you,
since you drove none of them, with harshness,
old or young, away as outcasts.]

980. ABCD, clia-sheanchuidh; E, cliar-sheanachaidh; F. cliath-sheanchaidh. The last (acc. to Dwelly) is “genealogical table.” clia-sheanchaidh is a corruption of an old collective term for “bards,” viz. cliar Sheanchain.

See article by W. J. Watson, in Celtic Review, IV, p. 80:

Seanchan was the name of the chief poet of the high king of Ireland in ancient times. The deposed bards took Seanchan as their patron, and called themselves Cliar-Sheanchain, the poets of Seanchan, the disciples of Seanchan.

Local tradition tells of a meeting Macintyre had with one cliar-sheanchain. According to the story told by Mr John Munro (Oban Times, 2nd April, 1904), the poet arrived cold and wet at Cuilfail Inn, Melfort, only to be informed that he could not be accommodated, as a trio of northern bards had settled in. By the rules

[TD 447]

of hospitality they could not be dislodged until a more capable disputant (deasbair bu mhurraiche) worsted them. The landlord, not recognising the poet, lamented that Ailean Buidhe, the only possible challenger, was not at home; but the visitor, fortified by two glasses of whisky, asked to be led to the bards. Duncan was greeted with the demand, “Stand back until we have heard your verse,” to which he replied—

<gai>Innsidh mise dhuibh mo rann
Mar mhaide cam an cuail,
Ma ’s dìreach leibh cuiribh ann
’S ma ’s cam leibh cumaibh uaibh.<eng>

The bards were unable to reply within the specified time, and Duncan delivered the final thrust with this stanza:

<gai>Ged a chuireadh na baird thuathach
An cinn cruaidh ri chéill’,
Bheirinn orra le eagal ’bhi
Gaolach air eaba a chéil’.<eng>

So the story ends with victory for the bard of Glen Orchy; and never again did a band of bards venture into Argyll.

The poet who was so opportunely away from home was Allan MacDougall, a farmer in Gleann Domhainn, Lochaweside. Seven songs of his are preserved in Turner’s Collection (1813), pp. 204–224. He would have been a doughty champion.


COLIN CAMPBELL OF GLENURE

Here we have another Campbell who was closely related to the Glenorchy family. Black Duncan of the Cowl, first baronet and seventh laird of Glenorchy, had a large family by two marriages; he had also several illegitimate children, one of whom was Patrick (1592–1678), the founder of the Barcaldine family. Patrick’s great-grandson, known as Pàra Dearg (1677–1738), was the fourth laird of Barcaldine. He was twice married. Iain Dubh (1700–77), son of the first marriage, was the fifth laird to 1775 when, owing to financial difficulties, he sold the estate to his half-brother.

Pàra’s second wife was Lucy Cameron, daughter of Sir Ewen Cameron of Lochiel. Of the children of this marriage we need note here but two—Colin of Glenure (1708–52), and Duncan of Glenure (1716–84). The latter bought the estate in 1775 and became sixth laird; the former is the subject of this poem.

Colin Campbell of Glenure held a commission in the Earl of Loudon’s regiment in the Forty-five, but he was in Aberdeen on

[TD 448]

the day of Culloden. In 1749 he married Janet Mackay, the granddaughter of the third Lord Reay; and in the same year he was appointed factor of the forfeited estates in Appin and Nether Lochaber.

Charles Stewart, fifth laird of Ardshiel, had escaped to France, leaving a natural brother, James, to look after his interests in Appin. James had played but a minor part in the rebellion and received the benefit of the Act of Indemnity; he lived on the farm of Glenduror, and proved himself not only a faithful and capable representative of his chief but also a very helpful intermediary between the tenants and the official factor. Even the removal of James from Glenduror to the inferior farm of Acharn did not cause a breach between him and the factor, although Glenduror was given to Campbell of Balliveolan, Glenure’s cousin.

In the summer of 1751 the factor granted the tenancy of a farm in Nether Lochaber to Cameron of Fassifern, another cousin. Though Cameron was in fact the highest bidder, an unsuccessful applicant laid a charge of favouritism against the factor, who might have lost his post if Lord Glenorchy had not testified to his loyalty.

Lord Glenorchy’s “memorial” on behalf of John Campbell of Barcaldine, factor of the Perth estate, and Colin Campbell of Glenure, factor of the Ardshiel estate and of “that part of the estate of late Cameron of Locheil which holds of the Duke of Gordon,” is included in the Bighouse Papers (Inv. G.S. Tr. XXII, p. 105). Both men had been recommended for appointment as factors of forfeited estates by Lord Glenorchy.

The following excerpt from the memorial gives an indication of the complaints made against Glenure:

“Mr. Campbell of Gleneure is, indeed, related by his mother to Cameron of Locheil’s family, and a handle was taken from thence to insinuate that he acted in everything in concert with Cameron of Fassefern, brother of late Locheil, who, not having been openly in the Rebellion, lives at home. But after the strictest Inquiry made by the Barons of the Exchequer in Scotland, his conduct was in every step approved, and the Falshood and Malice of his accusers evidently appeared.

“Mr Campbell of Gleneure is at the greatest variance with Cameron of Fassefern. He has brought several well affected Tenants into that part of the Estate of Locheil, of which he is Factor, and is daily bringing in more, which makes him hated by the people of that country, and is the true cause of his being accused privately by those who cannot do it openly.”

[TD 449]

With this incident fresh in his mind, Glenure, in the spring of 1752, gave notice of removal to certain tenants on the Ardshiel estate. James Stewart went to Edinburgh and endeavoured to stay proceedings but without success, and the unfortunate tenants were due to leave their farms on 15th May, 1752. Rumours of more evictions to come created intense excitement, and the stage was set for the Appin tragedy. At this critical moment Allan Breck Stewart appears on the scene. We find him flitting between Fasnacloich and Acharn, between Ballachulish and Callart, and watching the ferry. Allan was a deserter from the British army. He had joined the Jacobite army and fought at Culloden; afterwards he escaped to France and paid periodical visits to Appin, possibly to collect funds for the laird of Ardshiel. He had been brought up by his relative James Stewart.

On 11th May Glenure went to Fort William, carried out evictions on the estate of Mamore, and returned over the ferry at Ballachulish, without mishap, on 14th May. He had expected trouble in his mother’s country, and according to some accounts two parties were lying in wait for him, but no shot was fired. He was escorted by Stewart of Ballachulish from the ferry to the woods of Lettermore. Accompanied by his personal servant, a sheriff officer, and Mungo Campbell, who was a natural son of Iain Dubh, Glenure rode into the wood. He had not gone far when he was shot in the back, and soon afterwards he expired.

It is not necessary to discuss here the various accounts of this tragedy or the evidence against individuals. Some accounts name Donald Stewart, nephew of Stewart of Ballachulish, as the man who fired the Slinneanach, a long Spanish gun, which tradition links with the shooting; but a number of persons must have been involved in the plot, and the name of the marksman selected by them is of minor importance.

The murder of Colin of Glenure, brother of the laird of Barcaldine, was regarded by the Campbells as an insult and a challenge. A victim must be found, and James Stewart was seized and lodged in Fort William jail. The collection of evidence and the briefing of counsel was zealously undertaken by Iain Dubh, but there was no hurry for the trial. It was August before James knew the charge on which he was to be tried, and the trial was delayed till September, which ensured that the judges would be on the western circuit; that the case would be tried, not in Edinburgh, but in Inverary, with the Duke of Argyll, as Lord Justice General, presiding; and that the jury would be

[TD 450]

predominantly, if not wholly, composed of Campbells. The actual trial has been described as a mockery, with the verdict never in doubt. On 8th November, 1752, while a violent storm of wind and rain was raging, Seumas a’ Ghlinne was hanged at Cnap a’ Chaolais, near the scene of the shooting.

Colin Campbell of Glenure is described in some accounts of this tragedy as a kindly, easy-going man, so lenient to the tenants of forfeited estates that he was suspected of Jacobite sympathies. In other accounts he is represented as a harsh official who used his powers to harass the tenants, carried out evictions without mercy, gave farms to Campbells, and finally threatened to have all Camerons cleared out of Lochaber and Stewarts out of Appin.

In his elegy the poet is lavish in his praise. After discounting the conventional tributes of elegy we find much of genuine appreciation, affection and sorrow in the poem, together with much bitter feeling against the people of Appin. The ties of fosterage bound Donnchadh Bàn to Glenure: so the poem declares, though Colin Campbell was sixteen years older than the poet.

The reference to Glenure in MacDonald’s poem An Airc (AMD, p. 256) suggests that he was a man of such attractive personal qualities as to overcome Mac Mhaighstir Alasdair’s aversion to Hanoverians in general:

<gai>Ge toigh leam Cailean Ghlinn Iubhair
B’ fheàrr leam gum b’ iubhar ’s nach b’ fheàrna;
On thréig e nàdur a mhuinntreach,
’S gann a dh’ fhaodar cuim thoirt dà-san.<eng>

[Though I like Colin of Glenure,
I wish he were yew and not alder;
since he conformed not to his people’s nature
mercy can scarcely be extended to him.]

For detailed discussion of the Appin murder and the trial of James Stewart, see papers in Inv. G.S. Tr. by Sheriff J. R. N. MacPhail (XVI, p. 276, XXIV, p. 140), and by Mr Angus Matheson (XXXV, p. 343).

TITLE

A, Ghleanuir; B, Glinn-iuthair; CD, Ghlinn-iuthair; E. Ghlinn-iubhair; F, Ghleann Iubhair.

METRE

In this cumha of triple-stressed phrases the last stress in each half-strophe falls on ù throughout the whole poem—a severe

[TD 451]

restriction, involving the repeated use of monosyllables like ùr, ùir, cùl, thù, thùs.

TUNE

See Binneas nam Bàrd, Bk. I, pt. 1, p. 41; Orain a’ Mhòid, IX, p. 4.

TEXT

983. ABCDEF, gluasad am.

984. dùisg: state of waking; occurs mostly with prep. an combined with poss. pron. For an example of nom. see Turner (1813), p. 228:

<gai>Cha n’ eil mo chadal domh ach ciùirt,
’S cha n’ eil mo dhuisg ach cianail.<eng>

986. ABCDEF, rosgaibh; rasgaibh, common in speech, gives better rhyme. Cf. Turner (1813), p. 325:

<gai>Suil chorrach mar an dearcag,
Fo rasg a dh’ iadhas dluth.<eng>

1035. AB, euslain; CD, euslainnt; EF, euslaint.

1042. The Priory of Ardchattan, situated on the north shore of Loch Etive, about five miles east of Connel bridge, was founded in 1231 by Duncan MacDougall, Lord of Lorn, for monks of the order of Vallis Caulium. It was rich in endowments, and the prior was usually a MacDougall.

According to tradition, Wallace met chiefs of Western clans in the Priory in 1296, and Bruce held a council here in 1308.

The religious foundation was dissolved by the Crown in 1602, but Ardchattan continued to be used as a burial place. The building and lands became Campbell property, and the Priory was burned by the MacDonalds about 1642; but the walls of the cloister and the foundation of the tower may still be seen. Part of the prior’s house is incorporated in the modern house occupied by the proprietor.

St. Catán’s name is associated with a number of places in Bute and Argyll (CPNS, p. 277). (For a note on the history of Ardchattan Priory see Book of Barcaldine, p. 136.)

LAMENT FOR MAJOR ARCHIBALD CAMPBELL

The Campbells of Achallader were loyal henchmen to the Glenorchy and Breadalbane family. John (5th of Achallader) was chamberlain to the first and second earls, and occupied the house of Auchmore. He was succeeded in this important office by his son John, a man of great learning and culture.

Archibald was a younger brother of John, 6th laird. He received a commission in Keith’s and Campbell’s Highlanders on the recommendation of the Earl of Breadalbane. These regiments were embarked for Germany in 1760, soon after they were embodied, and joined the allied army commanded by Prince Ferdinand of Brunswick. They played a gallant part in successful actions at Warburg (11th July, 1760) and Zurenberg (5th August, 1760). On 5th October they were sent to reinforce the army of the Hereditary Prince of Brunswick. This army was defeated on 15th October by the Marquis de Castries at Kloster-Campen or Closter Camp (“Camphen” in Sketches). Captain Campbell was wounded in this battle.

The next engagement was at Fellinghausen (or Villinghausen) on 15th July, 1761, where the French under De Broglie were defeated by Prince Ferdinand.

(See Hassall, p. 274; but cf. F., p. 499.)

[TD 454]

It was in this action that Major Campbell was killed, “leading his men against the chosen troops of France. This officer’s talents, high spirit and military genius attracted great notice on one occasion, by a spirited dash, with a few of his Highlanders, against a detachment of the enemy, which had unexpectedly pushed forward to the quarters of General Griffin, afterwards Lord Howard of Walden; he dispersed five times his own number, and rescued General Griffin, who had been taken by the enemy. For this piece of service Captain Campbell was promoted, the week before he was killed.”

(From Sketches of the Highlanders, Vol. ii, p. 96.)

METRE

In this cumha the stanza may be arranged in four long lines, each having four stresses, and internal rhyme which may be continued on the same vowel for two, three, or four lines. The four lines have dactylic end-rhyme on a throughout the poem.

SONG TO A TAILOR’S FRIEND

This witty song no doubt raised many a laugh, especially among those who knew the individuals concerned, but its coarseness is inexcusable, and it was probably inspired by jealousy.

According to the MacNicol MS. (Inv. G.S. Tr., xxvii, p. 342), “MacNicol [Rev. Donald MacNicol] greatly assisted Duncan Ban in getting his poetry into shape. Duncan took his wife to Lismore and lived there for months, working with MacNicol at the poems … They quarrelled at last regarding a poem—The

[TD 456]

Tailor—which Duncan insisted on inserting contrary to the minister’s wishes, and this severance delayed the production of the work for more than a year.”

The reference might be to this poem or the next, and the minister of Lismore might well object to the publication of either. The fact that the poet omitted this short song from subsequent editions suggests that afterwards he was not proud of the composition.

Two young men are noted in the song. The next poem is a personal attack on Donald MacNaughton, a tailor, who, as the title explains, had taken his friend’s part and composed a lampoon on Macintyre. The story of the bardic strife thus appears to fall into four chapters:

(1) Courting visit by Donald and his friend MacBean.

(2) Song by Macintyre, mentioning Donald, but devoted mainly to MacBean.

(3) Song by Donald MacNaughton in dispraise of Macintyre.

(4) Macintyre’s violent attack on MacNaughton. (See next poem.)

TITLE

A, do charaid Táileir; shùiridh (shuirghidh on ‘Contents’ page of A).
E, do charaide Tàilear; shuiridh.
F, do charaid tailleir (“to a friend, a tailor”).
(B, C, D omit this song.)

METRE

A somewhat irregular òran.

The stanza can be arranged as four lines, each having six stresses and internal rhyme, which may be continued on the same vowel for three or four lines. The four lines of the stanza have end-rhyme on the last syllable (on à) in stanzas 1, 2, 5, 6, or on the penultimate syllable in stanzas 3, 4.

SONG TO THE TAILOR

MacNaughton’s song, written to the tune Gille nan car, began thus (E, p. 179):

<gai>“Gum b’ fhearr gu tarruinn bathainn thu
Na leagadh damh na cràice.”<eng>

Macintyre was evidently surprised as well as stung by the lampoon, and he lashes out in a cold fury through twenty-one stanzas, determined to show the tailor that he cannot hope to compete in sustained vituperation. The first song had the saving grace of humour, Rabelaisian though it be, but this poem is sheer abuse, a detailed list of defects and diseases, real or imaginary. It is a good example of bardic “satire” in which vilification ran riot, with little regard for fact or fairness. It gives ample proof of the poet’s command of the vocabulary of invective, but has little else to commend it to modern readers.

The poem was composed in 1752 (Highland Society Report on Ossian, p. 312).

[TD 458]

A, rinn eisean ann an adhbhar. BCD, Oran an Tàileir. E, Aoir an Tàilear, etc.

The phrases have two stresses and trochaic end-rhyme; the iambics rhyme throughout the poem on ò. The last phrase with the additional iambic is repeated in the stanza.

A SONG OF DALLIANCE

In The Literature of the Highlands, p. 52, the subject of this song is taken to be Màiri bhàn òg. But we have here a Mary MacNaughton who had brown hair and was apparently a dairymaid. Her lover promises to rescue her from the drudgery of the dairy, and the locality mentioned is Badour, where the poet had his first stalker’s cottage according to tradition. The girl described in this song is a merry, light-hearted lass, fond of singing and dancing, and quite unlike the placid and serious Mary whose praises are sung in the Song to his Bride.

METRE

A strophic measure in which each phrase has two stresses, the first three having trochaic end-rhyme which is repeated initially in the fourth line. The final stress is on ò throughout the poem.

Scheme: 3 (’s i Màiri Nic Neachdain) ri neach a tha beò.

The refrain is sung after each stanza.

TUNE

Orain a’ Mhòid, II, p. 9.

TEXT

Chorus: A, ’n inghean; B, nion.

1475. ABC, Neachduin; DEF, Neachdainn.

1480. A, eudan; C, eudain. For seall cf. 4667, 5704.

1482. ABC, feudainn.

1488. ABCD, cha d’ téid; also 1496. EF, bhuaile.

1489. Cf. Ri bleoghun cruibh ghùaill-fhinn. Turner (1809), p. 130.

1496. EF, Bhad-odhar. The form in the older editions suggests that the original name was Bad-dobhair.

1514. B, Nam bithidh i marrum; CDE, Nam bitheadh.

1533. ABCDE, thionnda’; F, thionndadh.

Here tionndadh is so indeterminate as to indicate an error in the first record.

A clue to the poet’s word is suggested by deoch-thunta, rendered ‘decanted drink’ in the vocabulary to the 1st and 3rd edns. of Alexander MacDonald’s poems.

The following quotations, noted by Mr Angus Matheson, illustrate the use of tunnadh (or tonnadh):

(a)
<gai>’S ged nach robh iad mar-ruinn,
Lathair anns a’ chollainn,
Chaidh an slàinte thonnadh
Le bonnanaich chalma.<eng>
Dàin agus Orain, p. 105 (Mrs MacPherson)

(b)
<gai>Gur salchar seit a’s urlair thu,
Lan sgeig a’s uilc a’s iombasaich;
Mur bhairrille ’n deigh a thunndaidh,
Cur sgum gu barr-iall bhrog.<eng>
Turner (1813), p. 82

(c)
<gai>Am fear caoimhneil, baigheil, duineil,
’Dh’ òladh deoch ’s cha b’ ann a cuman,
Ach a searrag a’ bheoil chuimir,
’S do thosgaidean air an uilinn;
’S iomadh stocach laidir, urrant’,
’Gheibheadh deoch an am an tunnaidh.<eng>
Maclean Bards, I, p. 212

The last line shows the gen. of vbl. noun tunnadh, but in older Gaelic the gen. was tunnta. This form would give rise to tunndaidh as in (b), and later, as the word passed out of speech, it would be confused with tionndadh.

From these examples tunnadh (or tonnadh) may be taken as ‘the act or process of draining, emptying’, etc.

Dwelly: tonnadh (1) splashing, pouring; (2) vomiting.

Dinn.: tonnadh (1) rising in waves; (2) vomiting.

1536. E, damhsa; F, dannsa.

1543. DE, -leumnach.

1550. BC, Dheanainn riut; DE, Dheannainn riut.

1551. AB, beadurra; CD, bead’ra.

1554. CDEF, docha.

[TD 461]

LOVE SONG

This is a typical Gaelic love song with the usual detailed description of the lady’s attractions; but a vein of teasing suggests that the song was meant, and understood, to be only a pretty compliment to a charming girl who had a higher social standing than the poet. She lived at Lochlairig west of Loch Voil and was presumably about the same age as Macintyre.

Dr Magnus MacLean (Literature of the Highlands, p. 53) assumes that the song was addressed to Màiri bhàn òg. She was certainly fair-haired, and she may have had clan or family ties with him (cf. 1569), though seòrsa may be used in a general sense; but Lochlairig is far from Mam Charraigh where the poet tells us he found his bride, nor could he have regarded anyone who lived at Inveroran as a comparative stranger (cf. 1621).

Rev. Alex. Stewart, LL.D. (1829–1901) says in his book, Nether Lochaber, p. 235, that the subject of this song was an ancestress of his, viz. Mary, sister of Alexander Stewart, 8th of Invernahyle. But this Mary married Nicol MacNicol, tacksman at Socach, Glen Orchy, as recorded by their son, Rev. Donald MacNicol, Lismore (Inv. G.S. Tr., XXVII, p. 341). As this son was born in 1735, when the poet was only 11 years old, it does not seem possible that this Mary Stewart could have been Màiri bhàn of Lochlairig.

Yet Dr Stewart was obviously referring to a tradition that connected the subject of this song with his own family. He is described (Modern Gaelic Bards, Pt. 1, p. 1) as descended from the Stewarts of Invernahyle and of Glenbuckie. (Glenbuckie runs south from the east end of Loch Voil.) His grandfather, Patrick Stewart, was schoolmaster in Ardgour, and he, according to information received from Dr Stewart’s descendants in Canada, married “Kate bhàn” of Invernahyle. The only person in the available records that fits is Catherine, daughter of Alexander Stewart.

Patrick’s grandfather, Robert Stewart, succeeded his father as tenant of Lettermore in Appin. He fought at Culloden and married a daughter of Stewart of Glenbuckie. She might have been the subject of this song. Alternatively, Robert’s son, James (Patrick’s father), who was tenant of Glenfinlas, which lies south of Loch Voil, may have married a Màiri bhàn from Lochlairig.

From the information available at present it can only be conjectured that Dr Stewart’s mistake arose through the confusion in tradition of the genealogy of different Stewart families.

(See Stewarts of Appin, 172, 184.)

[TD 462]

METRE

Séadna mheadhonach, the couplet having the formula (8³ + 7²). The last stressed vowel of the first line in the couplet rhymes with the first or second stressed vowel in the second line. The couplets rhyme in the stanza on ò throughout the whole poem.

TUNE

See A’ Chòisir Chiùil, p. 83; Orain a’ Mhòid, III, p. 3; Orain a’ Mhòid, XIII, p. 12.

TEXT

“Their rent is chiefly paid in kind, that is to say great part of it in several species arising from the product of the farm;

SONG TO HIS BRIDE

The Literature of the Highlands (p. 52) refers to the subject of this poem, as follows:

“As a young man, the bard … fell in love with a young girl of higher station and easier circumstances than his own. Her father, Robert Campbell, Claisgour, Blackmount, was an under-factor and freeholder. …”

According to Dr Calder she was said to be “a relation of the poet … in a somewhat better social position than her lover. Her father, a petty bailiff as well as keeper of a small wayside inn, which was later replaced by Inveroran Hotel, was named Nicol MacIntyre.” (F, p. xxvii.)

The latter account agrees with local tradition. The poet informs us that Màiri was descended from the men of Sleat, and in another poem he refers to the carpenter of Sleat as the eponym of the Macintyres.* She was evidently a serious-minded girl, gracious in manner, kindly in disposition and handsome in presence; and she proved to be a devoted helpmeet to the poet, first in their humble Highland cottage, and later in Edinburgh.

The poem has been much praised: by one authority it is considered to be “the finest love song in the Gaelic language.” It is certainly a serious and painstaking composition, and a sincere tribute to the young bride whose charms and qualities are described with excessive minuteness. The metre is elaborate and the diction dignified; the verse is ornate with some pleasing and naive conceits; but the poem as a whole lacks the fire and spontaneity of the genuine love lyric, though it has made Màiri bhàn òg the best-known female character in modern Gaelic poetry.


* See also p. xxxv.

[TD 464]

TITLE

A, Oran do Chéile; BC, Oran do Cheile Nuadh-phoisde; EF, Oran d’ a chéile Nuadh-pòste.

METRE

An intricate type of òran.

The stanza has four couplets; the first line of the couplet has four stresses, the last of which is on the penultimate syllable; the second line has three stresses, the last on the final vowel. The stressed vowel sequence in a typical couplet may be represented thus—a b b c / b c d. The d end-rhyme is used throughout the stanza, and is on é, ao or ua; the trochaic c end-rhyme of the first line is continued throughout the stanza in most cases. The elaborate rhyme scheme produces very musical verse.

TUNE

Footnote in E: “For the air, see Rev. Patrick Macdonald’s Collection of ‘Highland Vocal Airs,’ p. 21, No. 173.”

See Còisir Chiùil, p. 53; also S. Fraser Coll., No. 47.

[TD 466]

ar (vb.) plough; arach, ploughshare; arachd, ploughing; aradair, ploughman; araireachd, tillage (Colonsay). [Lat. arare, to plough.]

Cf. Ken. Mackenzie, Poems, p. 71:

Dheanainn cur a’s ar is buain dhi.

From Burt’s Letters, Vol. II, p. 123, Letter xx:

“Where the soil is deeper they plough with four of their little horses abreast: The manner this:

“Being thus ranked, they are divided by a small space into pairs, and the driver, or rather leader of the plough, having placed himself before them, holding the two innermost by their heads, to keep the couples asunder; he with his face towards the plough goes backward, observing, through the space between the horses, the way of the ploughshare.”

The man who led the horses was ceannaire. (In E.Ir. the leader was called cennairem, and the rear ploughman was tónairem.) See also Soc. Life Scot., p. 156.


SONG TO A FOSTER CHILD

According to the tradition quoted in F, p. 504, Isabel was the granddaughter of the tenant of Claisgour in Blackmount, said to be Robert, son of Campbell of Barcaldine. Lines 1784, 1787, 1789 tell us that she was of the Earl’s house and had a Cameron grandmother. Isabel’s father, therefore, might be a son of Pàra Dearg who married Lucy Cameron of Lochiel. (See note on Lament for Colin of Glenure.) But Pàra’s son, Robert, is described as “of Stirling and London.” He married a daughter of Major Cunningham of Boglas and died in 1776. It does not appear that he was ever a tenant in Blackmount.

There is clearly some confusion in the tradition quoted in F. The Literature of the Highlands adds to the confusion by naming Robert Campbell, Claisgour, as the poet’s father-in-law.

The reference to Culloden is of some interest as a contemporary reference to the part played by the MacDonalds in the battle. The poet merely says that the right wing “was taken from them,” implying that they claimed or expected to be placed on the right. There is here no suggestion that they declined to advance as stated by Chambers (History of the Rebellion, p. 297): “… the Macdonalds withheld themselves on account of their removal to the left wing. According to the report of one of their officers, the clan not only resented this indignity, but considered it as omening evil fortune for the day. …”

See also History of the Highlands (Browne), Vol. III, p. 248.

[TD 467]

Sir John Macdonald’s Memoirs and Sullivan’s narrative, quoted in 1745 and After, show the incident in a different light; but the account of the battle given by these Irish officers is by no means an unbiased one. One fact seems clear: the Prince’s army was weakened by disunity and by lack of authority.

METRE

A somewhat irregular type of òran.

There is internal rhyme in each line, and the four lines in the stanza have end-rhyme on the last syllable, with the exception of the first stanza.

TEXT

Title: ABCDEF, altrom. O. Ir. altram, gen. altrama.

THE DARK MAID OF RANNOCH

This bright lyric was apparently inspired by an incident of which the poet had heard. One girl had her meagre savings stolen by another—perhaps a fellow servant—who hailed from Rannoch. Rannoch had a bad reputation as a haunt of robbers and ‘broken men.’

[TD 468]

In an age that had little coin in circulation the loss of even a small hoard was serious. In the first half of the (18th) century coins “were not sufficient for the currency needs of the country; gold was never seen; silver was exceedingly scarce, especially after all the Scots coinage had been called in subsequent to the Union. In default of Scots or English money, foreign coins were in ready use, and money which came from Holland, Spain and France was welcome. …

“Leg-dollars, rix-dollars, guilders, ducatoons were of service as home currency.” [Soc. Hist. Scot., p. 31.] A Leg-dollar was equivalent to £2 16s. Scots, and a rix-dollar to £2 18s. Scots. At the time of the Union £1 Scots was worth 1s. 8d.

TITLE

AB, Oran, mar gun deanadh Nian é do Niain eile. CDE, Oran mar gu’n deanadh Nighean e do Nighin eile. F, Oran, mar gu’n deanadh Nighean e do Nighinn eile.

METRE

Ochdfhoclach mór corranach.

Formula for the half-stanza: 3 x 6² + 5¹. The second and third lines have end rhyme, on ì with few exceptions, and the fourth lines rhyme throughout on à.

TUNE

’S e rinn an eucoir a’ bhéisd a thug uam thu,
’S tha mi fo ghruaim o mhaduinn Dimàirt.<eng>

SONG TO A EWE

“Waulking” songs were sung by a team of young women when they were fulling cloth. They were seated at a hurdle of boards or wickerwork, on which the new tweed or blanket was pushed from side to side by hand. In some districts the girls sat at opposite sides of the hurdle and pounded the cloth with bare feet. Burt mentions this method in his Letters. The movement was regulated by music: one girl sang the verse and all joined in the chorus. The verses were couplets or single lines, and topical verses were often introduced.

The first part of this serio-comic song records the merits of the ewe and her untimely end; the second describes the bard’s projected tour of wool thigging; the third section notes the various stages of manufacture, and in the closing verses the bard once more laments his loss. The poem is not too long for a waulking session, the local references would maintain interest, and the humour could appeal to those who knew the persons and places mentioned.

All the places named in the song are in Glen Etive except Narrachan (An Arthar), which lay some distance up Glen Kinglass, and Coire Uanain, on the south side of Loch Linnhe between Corran Ferry and Fort William. Alexander MacDonald of Dalness, who died in 1726, married Jean, daughter of Dugald MacLachlan of Coire Uanain, and three of their sons lived for some time at this farm. Susan, who gave the ewe to the poet, was probably related to this family (F, p. xxvii).

Glen Etive runs north-east from the head of Loch Etive and to the south of Buachaill Eite; Làirig Ghartain lies between the two ‘Shepherds,’ and Allt Ghartain flows in this valley, to join the River Etive at Dalness. On the south side of the river, opposite Dalness, lies Inbhir Ghìneachd (see Map A).

If we follow the road down the glen we pass Inbhir Chàrnain on the right, 2½ miles from Dalness; on the opposite bank of the river we see Gleann Ceitilein, where Taigh na Sròine was situated. Continuing down the glen for a mile or so we pass Druim a’ Chòthais, and after another mile we arrive at Guala Chuilinn at the head of Loch Etive. Kinlochetive lies on the opposite side of the river.

Faoighe (thigging). For historical notes on this practice see Scot. Verse, Dean of Lismore, pp. 272–5.

“The practice (of thigging) was of old standing among the Gaelic people, and is not to be equated with common

[TD 470]

mendicancy. It entailed no stigma upon the craver, and was by no means confined to the lower classes of society. On the other hand, refusal of the thing craved is represented as extremely dishonouring to the person refusing.”

METRE

The line has four stresses and the couplets rhyme on a throughout the poem.

TUNE

See An Deò Gréine, March 1921.

ELEGY ON A COCK

The shooting of the farmyard cock that was mistaken for a kite in the gloaming would afford much amusement to a rural community, and the incident could not be allowed to pass unsung. In this cheerful elegy Macintyre’s humour is seen at its best. Pàraig (2087) was Peter Fletcher, Inverveigh, Glen Orchy. So the editor was informed by Rev. Angus Fletcher of Skipness, Peter’s great-great-grandson.

All over the land cock-fighting was a popular, if cruel, sport. In towns men of all classes found pleasure in visiting cockpits; and in rural districts every boy that possessed a fighting bird would bring it to school on Shrove Tuesday, when the cocks were matched against each other. The dead birds became the property of the poorly paid schoolmaster. (Soc. Life Scot., p. 430.)

A cock that refused battle or gave up the fight was called a fugie (Lat. fugio, I flee). This word survives in Gaelic as fùidsidh, a weak or incompetent person; a coward.

[TD 473]

METRE

The couplet has the formula (6² + 8²), and couplets rhyme throughout on a.

In the first quatrain of each stanza, except the first, the first three lines have trochaic end-rhyme, repeated internally in the fourth line.

A HUNTING FIASCO

This is a very popular song, humorously describing the occasional disappointments and the hardships of a gamekeeper’s life. But the poet ends on a characteristic note of optimism.

SONG TO GLEN ORCHY

Mr William MacKenzie, who wrote a series of articles in the Oban Times in 1904 on the “Land of Donnchadh Bàn,” was unable to identify Torr a’ Mhuilt. Dr Calder (F, p. 495) took it to be Multer’s Hill. Moutrie’s Hill (Memorials of Edinburgh, p. 250) was the site now occupied by James’ Square. The poet may have lived in, or frequented, this quarter of the capital; in any case he felt that he was far from home, like the Mull bard who sang:

<gai>Ged tha mi ’m fhògarrach cian air m’ aineol
’Sa’ Chaisteal Nuadh. …<eng>

The exile’s nostalgia, so often expressed in modern Gaelic songs, rarely appears in Macintyre’s poetry; but on this Sunday his thoughts take wing to the lovely vale of Orchy and the parish church at Dalmally.

This was Pennant’s description of the glen:

“The country opens up on approaching Glen Urqhie, a pretty valley, well cultivated, fertile in corn, the sides adorned with numbers of pretty groves, and the middle watered by the river Urqhie: the church is seated on a knowl in a large isle formed by the river.” Rev. Joseph Macintyre, D.D., was minister of the united parishes of Glenorchy and Innishail from 1765 to 1823. He wrote the article on his parish in the Statistical Account (1793), Vol. 8, p. 335, and notes that the church was “neither uncomfortable nor incommodious.” But Rev. Patrick MacDonald, famous as a collector of Gaelic tunes, records that his church at Kilmore (near Oban) is “poorly seated,” and that in the Kirk of Kilbride the seats are “equally bad.” Indeed, in many places worshippers brought their own stools, and those who could afford it had pews or “desks” built for themselves.

According to Rev. Joseph Macintyre, dìseart or Dysart represented Tì ’s àirde; but every clachan could be so designated. The

[TD 475]

word dìseart is from Lat. desertum, a desert; hence a retreat or hermitage. Clachan an Dìseirt was the church; Dal Mhàillidh, where the inn stood, was situated on higher ground to the south of the river.

Cf. John MacGregor, Poems, p. 116: ’n uair a rainaig sinn Dalmhàli.

TITLE

A (Contents page), Oran do Ghleann-urchaidh; A (text), Oran Ghlean-urchaidh; B, Ghlin-; CD, Ghlinn-; E, Ghlinn’-Urchaidh; F, Oran Ghleann Urchaidh.

METRE

The couplet has the formula (8³ + 6¹). The four couplets in the stanza rhyme on u or ò.

TEXT

ann is often used for an (prep.), e.g. ann dasg, 2225.

2224. E, mhiorbhailteich; F, mhìorbhailteich.

2225. cur: possibly the general arrangement or design.

The pews were not decorated by covering or carving; but the regular rows, arranged to conform to the octagonal shape of the building, would seem elegant enough by comparison with the primitive cottage seats.

2235. AB, co’-chaoin gheal; CD, co chaoin gheal.

2239. AB, spúrt; CD, spuirt.

There may be a reference here to “penny weddings.” A contribution, originally a penny, was made by guests to provide music, liquor and food at rural weddings. These weddings were often occasions for brawling, and many adverse comments on them are found in kirk session records.

2240. AEF, Fonn-cheol; BCD, Fonn cheol.

2241. E, mu sgur.

2243. cuir: plur. of car, bar, variation (in music).

<gai>’Nuair a ghlacadh tu do Chlàrsach,
’S a bhiodh tu ’g a gleusadh lamh rium,
Cha mhath a thuigte le h-umaidh
Do chuir chiuil-sa, ’s mo ghabhail dhàn-sa.<eng>
(Stewarts, p. 359)

2252. Common plur. of frìth is frìthean.

2272. AB, a stigh-thairne = i’s tigh-thairne.

i’s (in’s) represents the O. Ir. prep. i n- with s from the art. Before nouns with initial d or t, we find i’s, is, e.g.: is tìr, ’s dùthaich.

Alehouses where ‘twopenny ale’ was sold were to be found in most villages. Whisky became popular in the second half of the 18th century.

2276. A, fhaicteadh; CD, fhaic’te.


MISTY CORRIE

Song to Coire a’ Cheathaich is one of Macintyre’s most important poems. A beloved scene is depicted with affectionate touches, and each section of the picture is filled in with meticulous care. All the details, except perhaps the salmon, have been noted by the poet within the corrie or its environs, and the discomforts of heavy rains and frequent mists are forgotten when he thinks of sunny mornings and happy days on the hills.

No doubt other corries would have served the poet equally well for a subject, but personal feeling is too strong to let the poem become a wholly objective exercise. This poem is, indeed, an outstanding example of Gaelic descriptive poetry, with its musical rhymes, ornate diction and completeness of survey.

This, the most famous corrie in Gaelic literature, lies on the south-east slope of Creag Mhór, some six miles north of Crianlaraich, and about five miles east of the southern spur of Ben

[TD 477]

Dobhrain. It is within the Forest of Mamlorn and is drained by Allt Cheathaich, a stream that flows into the River Lochay, a little to the north of Allt Làirig Mhic Bhàidein; the latter is a tributary of Abhainn Ghlas, which flows into the west end of Loch Lyon (see Map B).

Two other tributaries of the River Lochay may be noted—Allt Bad a’ Mhàim, flowing parallel to Allt Cheathaich, less than a mile to the east, and Allt Bad Odhar, which joins the Lochay at a point three-quarters of a mile further east. Here is ‘Badour,’ where according to tradition the keeper’s cottage, occupied by the poet, was situated, some ten miles west of Killin.

METRE

Ochdfhoclach beag, in which the metrical unit has the formula (3 x 5² + 4¹). Four of these units form a stanza, usually arranged in eight lines.

The trochaic end-rhyme of the first three phrases is repeated internally in the fourth. The final stressed vowels in the sections correspond generally, but the rhyme here is defective in most stanzas, e.g. fonn, leam, grinn, mang. This defect is masked by the sound of the trochaic rhymes echoing down the stanza.

TUNE

See Orain a’ Mhòid, XVI, p. 11. For the earliest published set, see Patrick MacDonald’s Collection, No. 132.

2293. suim: Sc. soum, the number of cattle one could put on the common pasture of farm or village. When several tenants cultivated one farm, the farm was ‘soumed’ by dividing the total number of cattle the farm could pasture by the number of tenants. A mixed soum was reckoned by an agreed formula for each locality.

Rev. Duncan Stewart, who wrote the description of Balquidder in the Statistical Account (Vol. VI, p. 88), noted that 8 sheep = 1 horse = 2 cows.

Pennant (Tour, Vol. II, p. 274) found in Rum that “every pennyland is restricted to 28 sums of cattle; one milch cow or ten sheep is reckoned a sum; a horse is reckoned as two sums.” The system is sumachadh in Skye; the term cosgoradh was used in Lewis.

[TD 480]

LAMENT FOR MISTY CORRIE

The poet was greatly distressed by what he saw and heard on a visit to his former haunts, and blames MacEwen’s stewardship. Just as a good king was of old associated with prosperity, so the deterioration of the corrie is attributed to misrule. That MacEwen was responsible for the shrinking of waters, the decay of timber, and the disappearance of all game is but a poet’s conceit; but nevertheless the poet suggests that no improvement can be expected until the tàcharan is displaced.

Dr Calder (F, p. 489) quotes a persistent tradition “that the tàcharan was none other than Lord Glenorchy, who was undoubtedly unpopular, and had, from whatever reason, to flee the country and live in London, and against whose vote at the election of the Scots Peers there was a protest by Lord Saltoun in 1721.” This apparently refers to the election in 1721 of the second Earl of Breadalbane as a Scottish representative peer. The right of the Earl to the peerage was questioned on the ground that his elder brother was still alive. His son, styled Lord Glenorchy, was the capable diplomat and politician, who took over the management of the Breadalbane estates in 1745 and became the third Earl after his father’s death in 1752; and there could be no doubt about the third Earl’s heir, as his son John, Lord Glenorchy, was alive in 1768 when the poem was published. The situation that arose after the death of Lord Glenorchy in 1771 need not be considered here.

It is improbable that Macintyre would refer to the aged second Earl, his own first employer, as a tàcharan, or that, if the poem was composed after 1752, he would wish the third Earl displaced. It would thus appear that the tàcharan was MacEwen.

The following extract from Dr Alexander Carmichael’s papers, supplied by Mr Angus Matheson, throws light on MacEwen’s occupation (2459):

“Every crofter under a tighearna or fear baile was bound to give him a fat hen on Latha Samhna. This was called cearc fearainn, and the tax was càin nan cearc, the cain or tax of the hens. On some estates a dozen or two dozen eggs must go with the hen. …

“On some estates a man was appointed to collect the hens. He was called am maor chearc, the hen-bailiff, and was held in great contempt. …” Cf. 1609 note.

MacEwen had apparently held this unpopular office at Taymouth or Finlarig. He had now been put in charge of a

[TD 481]

deer forest, a position for which he was quite unfitted, according to the poet. (See quotation from Records of Argyll in Introduction, p. xxv.)

On the other hand, ‘Patrick’s race’ had a great reputation for prowess in sport. According to tradition, Patrick was the elder son of Lord Ormelie, who with his family retired to Auch, south of Ben Dobhrain, after the battle of Sheriffmuir.

Patrick married Jean MacNab, and their son Duncan, like his forbears, was an ardent Jacobite who joined Prince Charles abroad after the battle of Culloden. (See notes on Song to Lord Glenorchy; Famed Breadalbane, p. 176; F, pp. 489, 490; Statement of the Breadalbane Case, pp. 15, 19, 21.)

METRE

A cumha measure in which the unit may be represented thus: 3 (’s duilich leam an càradh) ’sa’ Bhràighe seo thall. The phrases have two stresses; the first three have trochaic end-rhyme, which is repeated initially in the fourth. Four units make a stanza, and the final stressed vowels (a or ù) correspond throughout the stanza.

In a long poem the scheme tends to produce monotony, especially if repeated use is made of the same word for end-rhyme.

TUNE

As noted in A, the poem was composed to the tune “The Flowers of Edinburgh.” Now the modern air of that name is well known as a Scottish Country Dance tune, which is quite unsuitable for a lament. But Rev. William Matheson gives an explanation in the following notes from the Journal of the English Folk Song and Folk Dance Society, Vol. III, No. 2, p. 123:

“‘The Flowers (Magistrates) of Edinburgh,’ an old Scots measure, appeared in Oswald’s Curious Collection of Scots Tunes, published in London in 1742, as My love is bonny when she smiles on me. Oswald gives a ‘slightly better version’ in his Caledonia Pocket Companion (III, 19), as ‘The Flowers of Edinburgh.’”

Stenhouse says it became a fashionable Scottish measure (a kind of 4/4 hornpipe) about 1740. The older, slower form would be familiar to the poet, and the original name was retained by the editor. A Jacobite song, To your arms, to your arms, my bonny Highland lads, was set to the tune at a later date. (See Jacobite Songs and Ballads, p. 202.)

[TD 482]

TEXT

2422. A, caramh; B, caradh.

2424. A, am araich; B, am arach; CDF, am àrach; E, gam àrach. The phrase greis am àrach occurs also in Cead deireannach nam Beann. The idiom commonly indicates occupation or condition, e.g. greis am iasgair; am thruaghan; am aonar.

Thus àireach for àrach would be a simple emendation in both instances, if the meaning of àireach could be extended to include ‘gamekeeper’ or ‘forester’; but the word is apparently restricted to ‘cowherd’ or ‘byreman,’ and was sometimes used as a term of contempt.

A common line in modern Gaelic songs is Far an d’ fhuair mi m’ àrach òg. Here àrach is the process of rearing or nurture. In older poetry the word has a rather wider meaning.

<gai>Fhuair sinn greis ’gar n-àrach
Aig MacLeòid a bha ’san Dùn<eng>
(Sàr Obair, p. 89)

(For a time we were maintained by MacLeod who was in Dunvegan.)

Similarly, Bàrd Loch nan Eala, referring to the Campbells:

<gai>Gun fhios nach gabh iad ardan rium
Am fine dh’ araich riamh mi.<eng>
(Sàr Obair, p. 340)

So Turner (1809), p. 73:

<gai>Ge d’ thà mise an tràsa / Da m’ àrach fad uait<eng>

(Though at present I subsist far away from thee.)

If Macintyre used the word in the sense of ‘subsistence’ we might expect ’gam àrach or de m’ àrach. The latter is represented in the text where a = de.

2506. ABCDF, na prisein; E, na pris sin.

2522. Plant Names, p. 4, quotes and translates thus:

<gai>Feur lochain is tàchair, / An cinn an duilleag bhàite.<eng>

“Water, grass, and algae, / where the water-lily grows.”

Metre and sense rule out this reading.

(a) feurlochan is a compound noun with stress on the first syllable;

(b) tàchair is associated with feurlochain;

(c) an cinn (in which grows) points to (a) and (b) as the habitat of the water-lily.

Hence the dict. meanings of tàchair (algae, water-weed) based on this line do not suit.

F, p. 523, notes tàchair, slow current, as of water issuing from a loch.

Kirk’s vocab. (SGS, Vol. V, p. 92) gives tachran, brook.

2526. glumagan; plur.

2529. ’na refers to sùghan.

<gai>An rathad cha b’ léir dhomh
’Us leum mi ’s an staing.<eng>
(Oranaiche, p. 362)

2536. E, sail-chuaich.

2544. AB, thur leam; E, air leam.

2547. tìorail: kindly, sheltered (of lands); cosy, coddled (of people).

MacEwen, who had never wandered far from the kitchen fire, had become a forester. His very presence on the hills is an offence.

SONG OF SUMMER

No Gaelic song inspired Alexander MacDonald to write his poems on Summer and Winter: these subjects were apparently suggested by the publication of Thomson’s Seasons, at intervals between 1726 and 1730, though there is no similarity of treatment between the Gaelic and the English poems. MacDonald in his Song to Summer imitated the metre and the first verse of a song included in Vol. 1 of Ramsay’s Tea Table Miscellany.

MacDonald’s Summer was in turn imitated by Rob Donn in his Song to Winter; Ewen MacLachlan wrote poems on the four seasons; William Ross wrote on Summer and Dugald Buchanan a didactic poem on Winter.

Macintyre’s Song to Summer may be regarded as a trial of poetic strength. He chooses a stately Gaelic metre, and rings the changes on rhyme sequence, to produce an intricate pattern, at least as difficult as MacDonald’s; and if a long series of adjectives be a sign of power, he will not be found wanting. In this, the most formal of his ‘nature’ poems, he would fain take a wider survey and escape from the confines of specific localities, but he does not move far from the Highland scene. Song to Summer shows Macintyre’s wide vocabulary skilfully used to produce the most pleasing cadences, though the sense may not always be clear. Simple things are described, but the language is not simple; some of the words have passed out of common use, there are phrases of uncertain meaning, and descriptions are over-elaborated; but the poem is a noble structure, not excelled in style by any of the Gaelic poems on Summer.

TUNE

Patrick MacDonald Coll. No. 21. As for Oran do Mhac Mhic Ailein (Sàr Obair, p. 75).

METRE

Druimne suithe na bairdne.

The couplet has the formula (8³ + 8³); a line may require a word from the next line for metre. The stanza falls into two quatrains, X and Y, having different schemes of end-rhyme; the rhyme sequence in X is (abab), and in Y (aaab). In both quatrains

[TD 485]

the a-rhyme is repeated internally in the b line, and the b-rhyme is on ua or ò throughout the poem. Some lines have internal rhyme.

The two types are combined in every possible way, X + X, X + Y, Y + X, Y + Y.

2748–9. The interpretation of this couplet is uncertain.

leòlaichean: “probably trollius europaeus (globe flower), from òl, òlachan, drink, drinking. Children frequently use the globe flower as a drinking cup.” (See Plant Names, p. 22.)

This conjectured derivation has no support. If flowers or herbs were meant, the normal construction would be: a’ trusadh leòlaichean is a’ buain chòrr. The absence of a conjunction makes 2749 an explanation, or an example, of 2748.

Rev. C. Robertson (SGS, Vol. I, p. 102) notes the use of lòlagan for playthings in certain districts, and cites leòlag “babery,” from Dwelly.

2749. còrr = carra-meille (orobus tuberosus), according to Plant Names, p. 22.

Dwelly notes corr-mheille, corr-meille, corra-meille, plur. corrcha-meille; also carraicean, carrachan, with meaning: tuberous bitter vetch, wild liquorice, heath pease.

Cf. Dinn. carra mhilis.

(corr-, corra-, carra- in names of plants may be confused with caor-, caora- (berry), e.g. corra-mhèag, whortleberry, heath-berry (Lewis).)

Pennant—Tour, 1769, p. 310, writes of Skye, “The roots of orobus tuberosus, the car-meille or carmel of the Highlanders, are in high esteem in this and other islands. They sometimes chew and make fermented liquor with them.”

Plant Names, p. 22, quoting Lightfoot:

The Highlanders have a great esteem for the tubercles of the roots; they dry them and chew them to give a better relish to their whisky. They also affirm that they are good against most diseases of the thorax, and that by the use of them they are enabled to repel hunger and thirst for a long time. In Breadalbane and Ross-shire they sometimes bruise them and steep them in water, and make an agreeable fermented liquor with them, called cairm. …

[TD 488]

EF, bhrùinceach. The form in B suggests that the adj. may have been bruthainneach, sultry.

2752. ABCDF, orra; E, orr’.

<gai>Is paidearan geal dlùth-chneap
De ’n driùchd ghorm air an fheur.<eng>

2765. ABCDEF, g’ a.


PRAISE OF BEN DOBHRAIN

Looming over Druim Liaghart, Ben Dobhrain was familiar to the poet from his infancy. It is not so impressive as other mountains he knew, but it was one of the hills of home, and the haunt of deer; and for a descriptive poem such as this one mountain will serve as well as another for theme or title. So did the little brook, Allt an t-Siùcair, provide a subject for Mac Mhaighstir Alasdair.

Ben Dobhrain is portrayed in its summer dress, with much detail and real feeling; corries and crags, springs and streams are named affectionately, but the poet is mainly interested in the deer that roam over the mountain. Red deer and fallow deer are described with kindly understanding, and only an experienced stalker could depict so vividly the final scenes of the hunt.

In this long poem there is some repetition and overlapping, but the variation of metre, with its close imitation of a pibroch, prevents monotony; and the poem as a whole is the greatest and most original of Macintyre’s compositions.

TITLE

AB, Oran Bein-Dourain; CD, Oran Beinn-dourain; EF, Moladh Beinn-Dòrain.
BCDEF, Air fonn, Piobaireachd.

TUNE

See Còisir a’ Mhòid II, pp. 29–31.

METRE

The metrical unit may be represented by (P + n x Q), where n is a variable number, P a quatrain of rhyming couplets, and Q a strophic stanza, the last line of which rhymes with the couplets of P.

In ùrlar, n is 3 as a rule; P has the formula 2 (5¹ + 5²); and Q is of the form (3 x 5¹ + 5²).

[TD 489]

In siubhal, n is variable; P has the formula 2 (6² + 6²); and Q, (3 x 6² + 6²).

In some cases Q has an additional couplet rhyming with the last line of P. This simple elaboration of the siubhal strophic stanza fits well into the pìobaireachd air.

In crùnludh, n is 7 in the first group and 4 in the others; P has the formula 2 (8³ + 7²), and Q has the form (3 x 8³ + 7¹).

2794. E, bidh.

spor, a flint, from Eng. spar.

<gai>Bha ’n spor bearnach geur tana,
Am beul snaip air dea’ theannadh.<eng>
(Turner (1809), p. 298)

2796. “There were various patterns of flint-locks. The type here described appears to have worked thus: The trigger (tarraing, 3248) pressed a spring or catch (iarann-earra, 3249), which released the ‘cock’ (snap, 2796); the head of the latter, often fancifully shaped, projected above the stock and had an opening or ‘jaws’ (beul-snaip, 2794 quotation), in which the flint (2794, 3252) was fixed with a screw (tarrann, 2795).

“When the ‘cock’ was released it sprang forward, causing the flint to strike the ‘hammer’ (òrd, 2797, 3253). The ‘hammer’ was really a steel cover-plate, with a vertical lug, over the flash-pan; it was knocked aside by the impact of the flint on the lug, and exposed the touch powder (deannag, 3255) at the moment the spark (srad, 3254) was struck.”
(Sàr Orain, p. 166)

2807. Pàdraig: Supposed to be the son of Lord Ormelie, eldest son of the first Earl of Breadalbane. “Patrick … it is said had a seat near Coire-chruiteir, where he sat directing the chase.” Statement of the Breadalbane Case, p.19.

See F, p. 489; also notes on “Lament for Misty Corrie.”

2815. ghuiniche sraonadh: Dwelly renders ‘keenest speed.’ This is an indeterminate expression, and other examples from this poem, noted in Dwelly, do not inspire confidence, e.g.:

Under meilich, line 3267 is rendered “without hollows or howes”; but under tòic, the translation is “without sound or motion.”

[TD 491]

Rhyme with gaoithe suggests that the poet’s word was sraoineadh.

(i)
(a) sraon (vb.): slip, turn, stumble (O. Ir. sróenim).
(b) sraon: deflection, error (Dinn.).
(c) sròin (vb.): deviate (Dwelly).
(d) sraonadh: slip, rush, impetus.

Cf. Dàin Iain Ghobha II, p. 119:
<gai>Chum bhith seasmhach ’s nach sraon e,<eng>
to be steadfast and not stumble.

(ii)
(a) sraonadh, sraonais, sròin: huff, offence (Dwelly).
(b) sraon: anger, distrust (Dinn.).
(c) sraonaiseach: huffy.

(iii)
(a) sròinich: breathe, snuff, snort.
(b) sròineis: a snorting, snuffing, etc.
(c) sròineiseach: huffy, given to snuffing, etc.

There is much confusion and overlapping in this series. The ‘speed’ meaning does not fit in with cuinnean; the other meanings, related to sròn, fall into two groups: ‘huff’ and ‘snuff’; and the latter is the more appropriate. It would be strange if the poet, in his detailed description of deer, had omitted reference to snuffing.

Cf. Turner (1809), p. 298:
<gai>Bha mi g ealadh mar dh fhaodain,
Dol an aghaidh na gaoithe,
Mun gabha tu sraona ad shroin.<eng>

I was crawling as I could, working up-wind, lest you should catch a sniff (of scent) in your nose.

(The meaning ‘snuff’ for sraon was suggested to the editor by the late Prof. J. C. Watson.)

3099. gun chuma: the sense is “without equal,” but cuma (noun) is shape, form, etc. The expression may perhaps be explained by the M.Ir. phrase i ccuma cháich, like the rest.

laoidhre = ladhaire or ladhra, gen. of ladhar (1) cleft in hoof; hoof, toe; (2) natural fork or promontory, land between two converging rivers or hills (Dinn.). On the six-inch map the shape of the area between the two streams may certainly be described as a shank terminating in a hoof.

3126. ABCD, mearrachdasach; E, mearachasach; F, mearachdasach.

Derivation from mearach + dasach or from mear + cas presents phonetic and semantic difficulties. Mr Angus Matheson suggests that form and meanings may be explained by the compound, mearghus (mear + gus), which would yield mearghusach > mearchasach or meargasach > mearcasach. (See RIA dict under mergus.)

gus, vigour, force, high spirits, strong feeling (Dinn., H.S.D.).

Turner (1809), p. 78: ’N cuirt an leoghuinn mheareasaich. (Eigg Coll., p. 64, mhearchdasaich.)

3130. Annaid: a church; in particular, a patron saint’s church.

O.Ir. andóit. This word is found in many place names, e.g. na h-Annaidean (Shader, Lewis); Achadh na h-Annaide in Glen Urquhart, Spean Bridge, Glen Lonan near Oban, etc. Chapels were commonly situated near a spring or stream, to which by association peculiar virtues were ascribed. Hence ìocshlainte 3136. (See CPNS, pp. 250–4; 436–7.)

3140. AB, fáruin; CD, fàruinn; F, fàrruinn; E, àrainn.

McB. has farruinn “pinnacle,” from far and rinn. The meaning may have been based on 3140; but in normal usage rinn is not a peak, but a promontory. More probably the word is àrainn (precincts), with prothetic f. (See 2443.)

Prof. T. F. O’Rahilly relates árainn to earlier *árand, derived from ad and vbl. stem rind-, ‘cut.’

Cf. Ir. teóra, gen. teórann, earlier tórann, tárann (O.Ir. tórand): marking off, delimitation. (See SGS, Vol. II, p. 16.)

3200. feadan:

(a) tube, pipe, gun barrel;
(b) chanter, flute, whistle (Sàr Orain, p. 209);
(c) a moorland stream;
(d) a gap between hills; wind-gap.

In West Ross feadan is a col or a narrow gap between hills, with or without a stream.

Near Coire Fhraoich, here described, there are two feadan names, where there are no permanent streams, viz. Feadan Garbh, half a mile to the north, and Feadan Mhart Dhonna, the same distance to the south. These high gaps are generally draughty places.

In Oranaiche, p. 343, note prep. ann:

<gai>iad ’n an luidhe gu cuanmhor / ann am feadanaibh luachrach a’s cìb.<eng>

3252. spor is fem.; hence ris refers to òrd.

3318. A, bhreisdlich.

3319. ABCD, deisimearachd; F, deismireachd.

deisimireachd, intricacy, complication, example. Ir. deisimreacht, proof, quotation; curiosity (Dinn.). O.Ir an example, a quotation. do béer desemmrecht dúib, I will give you an example (Wb. 12c35; Lang. Ann. Ulster, p. 169).

In Desiderius, deismireacht = ‘curiosidad.’


SONG TO THE GUN

This is one of the most popular of Macintyre’s songs, with its rousing chorus and lively tune. It may have been composed before he left Dalness, though it was not included in the first edition; in any case, the long tramps through passes and corries seem delightful in retrospect, each place has its own associations, and the poet is clearly happy in recalling name after name.

[TD 499]

Coire a’ Cheathaich in Glen Lochay and Coire a’ Chruiteir on Ben Dobhrain have been noted in other poems; east of the latter ben, and separated from it by the valley of Allt Chonghlais, lies Beinn a’ Chaisteil, linked by a high saddle, named Am Màm, to Beinn nam Fuaran; and, on the northern flank of the latter, Creag an Aprain stands due east of Ais an t-Sìthein (Map B).

Let us now follow the Tyndrum—Glencoe road to Kingshouse. From this point we note Stob na Créise, two and a half miles to the south-west; Meall a’ Bhùiridh, two miles due south, and Beinn a’ Chrùlaist, two miles to the north-west. Some eight miles east of this ben lies the long ridge of A’ Chruach, west of Loch Laidon; and ‘behind’ this ridge (on the north side) we note the Black Corries. These places are outside the area of Map A.

Half-way down the road from Kingshouse to Dalness we pass Clach an Tuairneir; from Dalness we look north-east to Làirig Ghartain, between the Shepherds of Etive, and we look south to Fàs Ghlaic on Ben Ceitlein. This ben is bounded on the east by Allt a’ Chaorrainn, and on the south side by Allt Cheitlein; Allt Coire Chaolain, flowing north-west, joins the former a mile above the Etive; Féith a’ Chaorrainn lies south-east of Ben Ceitlein; and Beinn Chaorach is situated south of Allt Cheitlein, which joins the Etive near Tòrr Uaine. (There is another Meall a’ Bhùiridh three miles west of Dalness.)

The poet called his gun after its previous owner.

In the 17th century there were Macintyres in Craignish, who believed that the surname “Clanntyre Vc Coshem wer of auld native men, servandis and dependaris to the house and surename of Clandule Cregnis alias Campbellis in Cregnis.” In 1612 they gave a bond of manrent to Ronald Campbell of Barrichbyan, their chief being called in the bond, Gillicallum McDonchie Vc Intyre Vc Coshem.
(Coll. de Rebus Albanicis, p. 206)

The name Osham me Intyar occurs in the list of Fencible men for Craignish and Braelorn in 1692.
(The Commons of Argyll, p. 36)

The poet, according to tradition, bought the gun from a kinsman.

METRE

The stanza has three triple-stressed phrases with end-rhyme on the penultimate vowel if long, or on the antepenultimate vowel if short. The fourth lines, also triple-stressed, have trochaic rhyme throughout the poem on ua.

[TD 500]

TUNE

Originally tune of Nian Tormaid (S. Fraser Coll. No. 65).

See Còisir Chiùil, p. 86, Còisir a’ Mhòid, I, p. 16.

The chorus is repeated after each stanza.

TITLE

DE, do’n ghunna; B, ga nainm; CD, ga ’n ainm; EF, d’ an.

ORAN DUTHCHA

This song was evidently composed some time after the poet had removed to Edinburgh. While he appreciates the advantages of town life, he is not quite happy; he recalls the active life and simple pleasures of his native glen, and he misses the friendliness of the Highland hamlet.

METRE

An à-poem.

The stanza consists of three double-stressed phrases having trochaic end-rhyme, followed by a fourth line, also of two stresses. The fourth lines have trochaic rhyme on à throughout.

The chorus is repeated after each stanza.

See Orain a’ Mhòid, IV, 6.
