RAINN GEARRADH-ARM

This short ode was composed on the occasion of a visit the poet made to Glen Noe, the ancestral home of his clan chief, when he was shown the seal bearing the Macintyre arms. Glen Noe is a small, lonely glen, lying north of Ben Cruachan and opening on to Loch Etive.

Although they never owned the glen, the Macintyres held Glen Noe for some five centuries, to 1810, at first from the Stewarts of Lorn, and afterwards from the Campbells of Glen Orchy and Breadalbane, being employed as foresters by those two clans. Inv. G.S. Tr., XVIII, p. 291.

According to one tradition concerning the origin of the Clan Macintyre, the carpenter of Sleat got the name Saor na h-òrdaig from the incident described in the poem.

For other traditional accounts, notes on the clan chiefs and their removal from Glen Noe, see Inv. G.S. Tr., XVIII, p. 290.

Ailean Dall refers to the traditional connection of the Macintyres with Skye thus:

<gai>Clann an t-Saoir bho thaobh Chruachain,
Bha cruadalach treun,
Ged chaill iad a’ chòir
Bh’ aig an seòrs’ ann an Sléibht’.<eng>
(Sàr Obair, p. 327)

The chief who welcomed the poet to Glen Noe was James (1727–99). His wife was a daughter of Duncan Campbell of Barcaldine, elder brother of Colin Campbell of Glenure.

[TD 502]

Rev. A. Maclean Sinclair includes James Macintyre of Glen Noe in his list of Gaelic bards, 1725–1830, and attributes to him three poems in Gillies’ Collection, all in dispraise of Dr Samuel Johnson.

(1) ORAN DO ’N OLLA SHASGUNNACH, SAMUEL JOHNSON

<gai>Tuiribh. Chorus
O mosgladh Mit a Ghāidheil,
A’s nochdadh Mit gu lāetheil,
Do’ n Fhocalair ro Strāiceil,
Nach fuigheal fochaid dh’ ā Sinn.

A’ Lunduinn tháinig Siar Oirn
Fear-turais na droch Scialachd,
Neo bhunailtach, gun Riasan,
Mar Ulabeist Mi-chiallach;
’S e fuileachdach gu Riasladh.<eng>

(Gillies, p. 173 (29 stanzas))

(2) It is not surprising to find James Macintyre expressing admiration for Rev. Donald NacNicol in a long poem entitled:

ORAN DO MR. DOMHNUL MACNEACAIL, MINISDEIR LIOSMOIR,

<gai>Eirson a ro scairtealachd agus a Chothaich e, ann Adhaigh an Olla Lunduinnich.

Tuiribh. Chorus
’S e Mai’sdir Dōmhnul Mac Neacail,
An Gaisgeach Cōmhraig tha reachdmhor,
Chuir Gasd’ ann ōrda an eachdraidh,
Thug feachdadh do ’n daormunn<eng>

(Gillies, p. 189 (27 stanzas))

(3) Dr Johnson’s description of the Highlanders as a rude, ignorant people, gave great offence to the scholarly chief of the Macintyres, as the following stanzas show:

<gai>Dh’ airis thu le briotal bōilich,
Nach aithne dhuinn Litir-fhōlum,
No Chann’ainn ann tric ar Cōmhradh,
Chuir ann Clō, no lēughadh.

. . . . .

Tha ionnsachadh leinn ro luachar,
’S e prionnsapal gach deadh-bhuaidh e;
G’ ar n ionnsaidh gabhaidh, ’s bheir uainn e
Gun uabhar, gun ēislein.

[TD 503]

Tha mis’ ann am fhia’nuis Mairionn,
Measg nan Ciad dhe’ ’n iarmad thairis,
Le ’m bu mhiann bhi ’g iarruidh barrachd,
Gach Bealach a thēid sinn.

Ge d’ tha mi sann am so chōmhnuidh
Ann sa Ghleann aig Ceann na Mōintich;
Tha mi ’n Geall eir Rann a’s Rōsg ann,
San Chōmhradh nach lēugh thu.<eng>

(Gillies, p. 182, 585 (36 stanzas))

Dr Johnson, it would appear, became an obsession with James Macintyre. Another poem on this subject is printed in Inv. G.S. Trans., XXII, p. 177, from the Maclagan MSS. “Le Seumas Mac-an-t Saoir, Fear a Ghleinne Nodha, 1775.”

One stanza may be quoted as an example of the style of this ‘satire’:

<gai>Gur tu ’n losgan sleamhuin tarrabhuigh
’S tu maigean tairgneadh nan dìgin;
Gur tu dearc-luachrach an fhàsaich
Re snàg ’s re màgaran mioltaich.<eng>

James Shaw (Bard Loch nan Eala) refers to the Chief’s attacks on Dr Johnson, thus:

<gai>’Nuair a thain’ an t-Olla Sasunnach
Thoirt maslaidh ’n aird an Iar so,
Gur tusa phill gu h-ullamh e,
’S tu b’ urrainn dhol g’ a dhianamh.<eng>

(Sàr Obair, p. 340)

Stanzas 1, 2, 3—snéadhbhairdne, 2 (8² + 4²)2 + 4;
Stanzas 4, 6—rannaigheachd bheag mhór 2 (8² + 8²)2 + 4;
Stanzas 5, 7—combine the two metres.

THE HIGHLAND GARB

This song should be read in conjunction with Oran do ’n Bhriogais. The Act of 1746 forbade the wearing of the Highland dress and the possession of arms; even the bagpipe, being classed as an instrument of war, was forbidden.

The Marquis of Graham, afterwards third Duke of Montrose, was largely responsible for the removal of this oppressive measure from the Statute Book. As a member of Parliament for Richmond, Yorkshire, he brought in the bill to repeal the Disarming Act in 1782, and had the satisfaction of carrying it without opposition.

Although the law had not been strictly enforced for some years, the formal repeal of the Act was an occasion of great joy to loyal clansmen, and inspired Macintyre to compose this excellent song, which expresses not only delight in the new freedom but also the pent-up indignation of a whole generation of Highlanders.

The following is the title of a poem by Donald MacKenzie, published by Chapman and Duncan in 1785 (Typographia, p. 243).

<gai>Oran Gairdeachais Dhomhnuill Mhic Coinnich
An 30 dhe ’n cheud mhios Fhobhair bhlia’nna 1784;
An latha Cuirm a’s Greadhnachais A Ghleidh e
maille ri Chairdean, tra’ fhuair e an Naigheachd
Ait, gu ’n d’ thug an Roigh agus a Pharlimaid, eir
an ais do shliochd nan Ceann-Feadhna na
Gaidhealtachd na h oighreachdan chaidh tho’irt
uatha bhlia’nna 1746, as leith an abhair
mhi-fhortanaich ann san do ghluais iad.
Agus bu mho’ide thog e Chroidhe ris a so Gu’n
d’ fhuair na Gaidheil gu leir, beagan roimhe
sin lan chomas an Eidheadh sinnsearrachd
a chaitheadh mar a b’ abhaist dhoibh.<eng>

[TD 505]

METRE

Rannaigheachd dialtach, 2 (7¹ + 7¹)2 + 4, but many lines do not fit the syllabic formula. In stressed metre the poem is an òran with rhyming couplets; each line has three stresses, and the last stressed vowel of the first line assonates with the middle (or the first, in three instances) stressed vowel of the second line. The first syllable of the couplet bears the stress. Thus the reading chuir sinne in 3548 must be rejected.

TUNE

Còisir a’ Mhòid, III, p. 49.

cadadh, tartan cloth … “doubtless from Eng. caddis, worsted, crewel work” (McB.); but this is represented by cados, cathdos, hose, stocking hose, kilt hose (Carmichael MSS).

cadadh may be referred to E. Ir. cadúd, wrapping. Cf. cadógh, cadágh, a wrap, a blanket (Dinn.). In Bardic Poetry, cadúdh, cadódh, cadádh = welding, hardening. H.S.D. gives cadadh “tartan … formerly steel, mail, greaves, defensive armour.”

SONG TO THE CLANS

Though the date 1782 was inserted in the title of the poem in the third edition, the restoration was not completed until January 1785 (see “Ode to Gaelic,” 1785). The event called for a poem of celebration, but the verses lack the fervour and force of the Song to the Highland Garb. Six stanzas of this song are general and would form a complete short poem; but the poet adds eleven stanzas in praise of selected clans. The subject matter of these stanzas is of little importance: apart from locality they could be interchanged without loss of accuracy.

TITLE

B, Oran, na’m Fineachan a fhuair Fearann o’n Ri.
CD, Oran na’m … Righ, 1782.
E, … am fearann air ais o’n Righ sa’ bhliadhna 1782.
F, As E, with ’s a’ for sa’.

METRE

Formula for couplet: (8² + 6²). The couplets rhyme throughout on ò (penultimate). This scheme leads to the repeated use of certain words and to some degree of monotony.

3651. After the battle of Glenfruin (February 1603), where the MacGregors defeated the Colquhouns who were supported by a number of burgesses from Dunbarton, the Privy Council ordained that “the name MacGregoure suld be altogidder abolisched, and that the haill persounes of that Clan sulde renounce thair name, and tak them some other name, and that thai nor nane of their posteritie sulde call thame selffis Gregoure or M’Gregoure thair efter under paine of deade.” (Famed Breadalbane, p. 133.)

The proscription was withdrawn by Act of Parliament in 1774.

The Robertson estates extended up Glen Erochy and over to Rannoch; their chiefs were buried at Dùn Alasdair, beside the Tummel.

3685. luchd-fògraidh. In such compounds luchd- usually means agents, e.g. luchd-càinidh, luchd-frithealaidh; but in some instances there may be ambiguity, e.g. luchd-eòlais (1) well-informed people or (2) acquaintances.

luchd-fògraidh could mean (a) those who suffered exile or (b) those who would banish the king.

Macintyre’s own Song to the King, and the decline of Jacobite sentiment after the Seven Years’ War, suggest that the word was used in the sense (a), although in the following quotation (Inv. G.S. Tr., XXVI, p. 241) the reference is clearly to those who had banished Charles:

<gai>Thug iad mionnan a’ Bhiobuill
A’ dol sios gu Allt Eireann,

Nach cuirt’ claidheabh an truaill leo,
’S am biodh buaidh le Righ Seurlas,

[TD 509]

’S gus am faict’ a luchd-fògraidh
’Dol ’s na còrdaibh ’na éirig.<eng>

THE OLD HIGHLAND WATCH

The Black Watch or Royal Highland Regiment began in 1725 as a rural police force organised by Wade, to prevent cattle-lifting. It consisted of six independent companies which were embodied in 1729 as part of the armed forces of the Crown. Ten years later four companies were added, and the ten combined to form the Forty-third Regiment, and a new tartan was designed for its uniform.

In 1744 the regiment was embarked for Flanders, and played a distinguished part in covering the retreat of Cumberland’s army at Fontenoy in August 1745. During the ‘Forty-five’ rising the regiment was stationed on the coast of Kent.

After the battle of Culloden, the British Government, alarmed at the successful French campaign in the Low Countries in 1746, sent an expedition to Brittany. The Black Watch took part in an unsuccessful attempt to capture L’Orient in September 1746, and fought minor actions at Hulst and South Beveland in May 1747. The war was brought to a close in 1748 by the treaty of Aix-la-Chapelle.

In 1749, owing to the reduction of the old Forty-second Regiment, the Black Watch became the Forty-second.

METRE

The stanza of 16 lines falls into four sections: the metrical unit has three phrases with trochaic rhyme, while the fourth lines have end-rhyme on é throughout. Each line has two stresses. The unit may be represented thus: 3 (na spealpan gun athadh) an rathad sin féin.

3838. CDEF, miann; mionn sùl was common in bardic poetry.

mionn: diadem, jewel; treasure, delight; something precious, distinguished or beautiful.

mionn súl Eireann i n-Ath Cliath. (Rel. Celt, II, p. 302.)

As mionn passed out of speech, mionn sùl would be understood as miann sùl. Cf. 2055.

3841. B, co’ducha; CDEF, còmhdacha.

Kilt and hose are mentioned; and coats would be the next item.

B possibly meant códucha = còtacha, cf. 2329.

3843. BCDF, breacana ’n.

3858. BCDE, ga’n gibhtean.

3865. a chlisge: the usual meaning ‘suddenly’ does not fit context; but in Perthshire the phrase meant ‘presently.’ (Inv. G. S. Tr., XXII, p. 18.)

3866. B, nois. This form is frequent in B; but the rhyme here clearly indicates the pronunciation.


THE ARGYLL REGIMENT

The ‘Argyle or Western Fencible Regiment’ was raised in 1778 by Lord Frederick Campbell, son of the Duke of Argyll. About the same time the Earl of Eglinton also applied for permission to raise a regiment of Fencible Highlanders, but it was feared that recruiting for the regiments of the line would be affected if two Fencible regiments were raised in the Western Highlands. It was, therefore, resolved that one regiment would be raised, partly in the Glasgow district and partly in Argyll and other Highland counties. Lord Frederick Campbell was appointed Colonel. Though raised for home defence, the regiment volunteered for service anywhere. It was disbanded in Glasgow in 1783. (Sketches of the Highlanders, II, p. 376.)

METRE

Formula for couplet: (8³ + 7²)—séadna mheadhonach.

The couplets rhyme throughout on ù (penultimate).

TUNE

See A’ Chòisir Chiùil, p. 59.

TEXT

[TD 512]

POEMS IN PRAISE OF GAELIC AND BAGPIPE

These six poems might nowadays be regarded as Mòd prize poems. In 1781 the London Highland Society arranged a piping competition to be held at the Falkirk Tryst. Prizes were offered for the best performance in each section of the syllabus:

(a) Salute, march, gathering, lament—as selected by competitors.

(b) Glasmheur—as prescribed by the adjudicators.

One item in the programme was the recitation of an original Gaelic poem, selected as the best of those submitted to the Society. It is to be regretted that the subject was not changed: even Macintyre, with all his resources of vocabulary and metrical variations, finds it difficult to produce new ideas on the same topic, year after year. This may account for some obscurities in thought and language to be found in the poems.

The following notes, based on the records of the London Highland Society, are taken from Angus MacKay’s Collection of Highland Pipe Music (Edinburgh, 1838), p. 9.

1781
“The Highland Society of London … being desirous that the ancient spirit of the Great Pipe … should be revived,

[TD 513]

were pleased to order Annual Prizes to be played for, and to be adjudged to the best performers on that instrument, who should appear as candidates at the Falkirk Tryst. The first prize to be a set of new Pipes, made by Hugh Robertson, Edinburgh, and forty merks Scots money; the second prize thirty merks; and the third the like sum.

“Some gentlemen as a deputation from the Society at Glasgow, and the agent from Edinburgh, made their appearance at Falkirk, the day preceding that appointed for the competition. They met on the following morning, and adjourned to the Mason Lodge; when, after hearing an excellent Gaelic poem recited by an old grey-headed bard, (which he composed for the occasion), in the presence of a select company of ladies and gentlemen, thirteen competing Pipers, and the maker of the Prize Pipes, the deputation and the agent proceeded to the election of a preses, and six gentlemen to be judges of the merits of the performers. … The competitors were conducted to a room … where they … drew lots for priority of performance. This done, the person who drew lot No. 1 was conducted by a private door, to a small court below the windows of the lodge, in such a manner that the judges could neither see nor know the particular performer. Each person was made to play four different tunes. …

“The judges almost unanimously awarded the first prize to Patrick MacGregor, Piper to Henry Balnaves, Esq. of Adradour, in the parish of Mullin and county of Perth; the second prize to Charles MacArthur, Piper to the Earl of Eglinton; and the third to John MacGregor, senior, aged 73, Piper to Lt.-Col. John Campbell of Glenlyon. …”

Oct. 1782
“The competition for the prizes given annually by the Highland Society of London, for the encouragement of performers on the ancient martial instrument of Scotland, was determined by a select jury of gentlemen, in the presence of a numerous and respectable audience. After a long trial … the first prize was voted to John MacAlister, first piper to the West Fencible Regiment; the second to John MacGregor, the father, then 74 years of age; and the third to John MacGregor the son.”

Oct. 1783
“At the Annual competition for prizes, given by the Highland Society of London, which was held at Falkirk; the first prize was adjudged to Neil MacLean, Piper to Major Campbell

[TD 514]

of Airds; the second prize to Archibald MacGregor, fourth son of John MacGregor, Piper to Colonel Campbell of Glenlyon, and the third to John MacGregor, Piper to the City Guard of Edinburgh. The bard Ma can T’sior* was introduced, and pronounced his annual Gaelic poem, in praise of the martial music and prowess of the Caledonians. … On the return of the pipers from Falkirk by Edinburgh, it was conceived by many gentlemen, that an exhibition of their abilities would be a very agreeable entertainment to the public; it was accordingly arranged that it should take place in Dunn’s Assembly Rooms. … During one of the intervals, the Bard Donn MacIntyre, in the City Guard, was introduced, who repeated a poem in the Gaelic, in praise of that language and ancient music of the great Pipes, which was much approved of by those who understood that language.”

Oct. 1784
“The annual competition … formerly held at the Falkirk Tryst was performed in the Assembly Hall, Edinburgh, before a numerous and fashionable audience. There were sixteen competitors, all of whom were esteemed good performers by the judges. The prizes were awarded as follows:

The first to John MacGregor, senior, from Fortingal; the second to Donald Fisher, (second piper to the Earl of Breadalbane) and the third to Dugald MacDougall, piper to Dugald MacDougall Esq., of Gallanach.”

There are similar notes for each year up to 1828, after which the competition was held triennially; but there is no mention of a bard or Gaelic poem after 1783.

4238. Fionn, son of Cumhall, son of Trenmhor, was leader of the Clan Baoisgne in his time. He is depicted as a famous warrior and a man of many parts.

<gai>Fionn File ba fer go nglór / is ris adeirmís Trénmhór;
fa tréine ar threise a chleas / gidh edh ón ba hainm oircheas.<eng>
(DF, p. 25)

“Fionn the Poet was a man of speech, it was he that we called Trenmhor; he rather excelled in the strength of his feats, nevertheless it was a fitting name.”

Goll, champion of the Clan Morna, had a family feud with Fionn, but they lived on friendly terms for many years. Goll is represented as a great warrior who for a time commanded the army of Conn Ceudchathach.

<gai>Goll mac Morna mór-uallach,
cenn na ccuradh rígh na ttriath.<eng>
(DF, p. 58)

Garadh was a prominent Fenian warrior who fought under Goll.

4240. air seòl calla:

(a) F renders ‘tamely,’ taking calla as càllaidh;

(b) with calla as gen. of call, the sense might be ‘in destructive mood.’

But in either case we should expect chluinneadh for chitheadh, as caismeachd is an alarm, war song, etc.

The translation given expresses a sentiment that is common in these prize poems.

4302. From the Parliamentary Register VII, 1782, p. 235:

17th June, 1782. The Marquis of Graham moved for leave to bring in a bill to repeal an act … which prohibited the wearing of the ancient Highland dress in the Highlands of Scotland. …

The motion was seconded by the Honourable Mr Fraser of Lovat. …

Archibald Campbell Fraser (1736–1815) inherited the family estates from his half-brother Simon, to whom the forfeited estates had been restored in 1774. Simon died in 1782, and Archibald succeeded him as M.P. for Inverness-shire. Neither brother was recognised as Lord Lovat.

On a memorial erected by himself in the family mausoleum at the old church of Kirkhill, some years before his death, Archibald enumerated among his public services his efforts to improve fisheries, agriculture and industry in the Highlands, his petition to abolish duties on salt and coal, and his co-operation with the Marquis of Graham to procure the restoration of the Highland garb.

VERSES ON THIRST

In an age of excessive indulgence in liquor, when heavy drinking was both common and fashionable, numerous songs of conviviality were composed. This short piece, while simple in language, is somewhat obscure in setting and sequence.

METRE

Formula for couplet is approximately (8² + 8²); couplets rhyme throughout on a (penultimate).

<gai>’S mairg nach giùlain’ an copan,
Gle shoc’rach ’s e dearr-lan,
’S ionunn sin ’s mar rinn mise,
Gus ’n do bhris mi fhein bearn as.<eng>

Here the stress on dearr-lan is clearly on the first syllable.

SONG TO BRANDY

This witty poem is a good example of convivial songs; it is simple in diction, lively in style and sufficiently bacchanalian in sentiment.

SONG TO THE BOTTLE

This is the most elaborate of Macintyre’s drinking songs. Unlike Alexander MacDonald’s “Bottle” song, it is not complicated by political opinions or propaganda. It is a clever, humorous composition and appropriately merry in the rollicking second half of each stanza.

METRE

The first four lines may be represented by the formula (3 x 6² + 5¹), and the second four lines similarly—Ochdfhoclach mór corranach. But the second half of the stanza does not fit this syllabic formula. If we arrange it in four long lines, as in editions B, C, D, each line has four stresses with the following rhyme scheme:

(1) The second and fourth lines rhyme with the 5¹ lines of the first half, on a, ea, or ai.

(2) The first and third lines have dactylic end-rhyme which is repeated internally in the second line and twice in the fourth line.

(3) The second stressed vowels (long) in the first three lines rhyme. This rhyme, e.g. tìr … thì … strìth, imitates a sustained note in piping.

TUNE

S. Fraser Coll. No. 61—Ciamar as urra’ sinn fuireach o’n dram—composed by John MacMurdo (or MacRae) of Kintail. See footnote on p. 31 and also p. 109, S. Fraser Coll.

ALASDAIR NAN STOP

This simple ditty, full of kindly humour, was composed in compliment to Alexander Campbell, a native of Glen Orchy, in whose house the bard had been hospitably entertained. Campbell, after a period of service in the army, kept a small public-house in Back Wynd, Glasgow.

Calum Breac, who had expressed some doubt regarding Alasdair’s prowess, was Malcolm MacNab, farmer in Barrachaistealain, which is close to the railway station at Dalmally. He was a friend of Duncan Macintyre and something of a bard himself. (See footnote to this poem, E, p. 117.)

The MacNabs of Dalmally were for many generations famous as smiths and armourers. In his record of his visit in 1768, Pennant says that the family had lived at the same place since 1440, the first of the craft “having been employed by the lady of Sir John Campbell who built the Castle of Kilchurn, when her husband was abroad.”

In 1780 Alexander MacNab, blacksmith at Dalmally, supplied Ossianic ballads to Thomas Ford Hill, who published them in the Gentleman’s Magazine in 1782.

Faujas St Fond, a French traveller, records that he was hospitably received at the smithy of Dalmally.

Robert Heron, whose Tour in Scotland was published in 1793, visited the MacNabs, as did Dr John Leyden in 1800. Both these travellers saw specimens of coat of mail, helmet and targe, wrought by MacNabs of former generations.

In the list of subscribers to the second edition (1790) we find the names of John MacNab; Malcolm MacNab, farmer; and Duncan MacNab, smith—all at “Barrachastlan.” Alexander Campbell, Back Wynd, Glasgow, was also a subscriber. Back Wynd ran parallel to, and half-way between, Saltmarket Street and Stockwell Street.

[TD 525]

METRE

The couplet is of the form (6² + 5¹), and the couplets rhyme.

As for the song Dh’ fhàg thu mi fo bhròn (S. Fraser Coll. No. 157).

HUGH THE PIPER

It was probably on his visit to the north-west to solicit subscriptions for the second edition of his poems that Macintyre fell foul of the ill-mannered piper. The poet had met with much kindness, as the list of subscribers shows, but Hugh, who had some pretensions to be a bard, made such a rude attack on the stranger that he invited ruthless retaliation. Modern taste would exclude several stanzas as scurrilous, but the poem is in the traditional style of bardic flyting.

LAMPOON ON ANNE

The poet was manifestly taken aback by the rude reception Anne of Cromarty gave him. If his allegations are to be accepted as true, she was indeed a disreputable character, without a redeeming feature in conduct or appearance. In such ‘satires,’ however, the most outrageous statements are made without regard for fact, and much may be discounted.

LAMENT FOR THE EARL OF BREADALBANE

After a notable career in politics and diplomacy, John, Lord Glenorchy, returned to Taymouth in 1745 and succeeded his father in 1752. He built the village and bridge of Kenmore; he continued his father’s schemes of afforestation and made Taymouth a very attractive place. (See note on Song to Lord Glenorchy, 469.)

His two sons predeceased him, and the third Earl was thus the last of the direct Glenorchy line. He died at the Palace of Holyrood House on 26th January, 1782, aged 86 years. (F, p. 491, gives 26th June, apparently from footnote in E to this poem; but see line 4769.)

Dr Calder suggests that the last stanza refers to Duncan Campbell (“Big Duncan of the Sword”), son of Patrick, elder son of Lord Ormelie who was disinherited by the first Earl. This Duncan was such a staunch Jacobite that even the promise of the earldom of Breadalbane, together with a free pardon from the Government, failed to change his loyalty to the lost Stuart cause. He went abroad to join the Prince and never returned.

The succession devolved on John, great-grandson of Colin of Mochaster, who was the second son of Sir Robert Campbell, 9th laird of Glenorchy. The fourth Earl was educated at Westminster and lived at Lausanne for some time. This may be the simple explanation of line 4818.

Sir Robert Campbell, 9th of Glenorchy

(1)
Sir John, 10th of Glenorchy
John, 1st Earl
John, 2nd Earl
John, 3rd Earl

(2)
Colin of Mochaster
Robert
Colin of Carwhin
John, 4th Earl

The remaining stanzas have couplets of the form (8² + 7¹), with the end-rhyme on ua or ò.

IONA

In Adamnan’s “Life of St Columba” the name of the island is Ioua insula. A misreading of u as n is responsible for the modern name. The Gaelic name is I or Hi (I Chaluim Chine), gen. Idhe.

St Columba settled in the island in A.D. 563, but the buildings—church, nunnery, chapels—were built much later than the poet indicates.

Rev. Dugald Campbell was inducted to the Mull parish of Kilfinichen and Kilvickeon in 1779. He was required to preach every sixth week in Iona. (See F, p. 507.)

CONCLUDING VERSES

This is the last poem in the second edition. It shows the poet in serious mood, contemplating man’s mortality, and expressing in simple form the theological teaching of his day.

The last stanza presents difficulties in text and interpretation.

SONG TO EDINBURGH

“By 1770 there were signs … of the approaching transformation of Edinburgh. … The town which had remained within its ancient bounds and walls for 250 years was becoming too circumscribed for its population. … About 1760 there had been erected squares of self-contained houses south of the town. … In 1772 the North Bridge was finished. … Plans by that time had been formed for streets on the other side of the ‘Nor’ Loch’ (the lake or swamp now the Princes Street Gardens), but slow progress was made till 1780. …

“Town and town life underwent a revolution. … The sedan chairs were becoming worn out, like the chairmen who had carried in them so many fair occupants. … The chairs were being discarded for hackney coaches that drove swiftly along handsome though unfinished streets.” (Soc. Life Scot., p. 124)

The poet would have been interested in the removal of the low, dingy building that had been the headquarters of the city guard. It was situated in the High Street, opposite the Tolbooth. In 1785 the ‘guard’ was transferred to the New Assembly Close, but when neighbouring proprietors objected to the presence of the “town rottens” the ancient force retreated to premises in the Tolbooth. Here they lingered for a few years, and the last of them was placed on the town’s pension list in 1817.

(See Wilson’s Memorials, p. 248)

METRE

Couplets of the form (7³ + 6¹) rhyme throughout the stanza.

SONG TO THE FOXES

The change from the stunted, delicate Highland sheep to larger and sturdier breeds that yielded more meat and wool was in progress by the middle of the eighteenth century. Sheep farms extended into hilly country where deer used to roam; and as shelter for stock could be found only in the glens, many small crofting communities had to leave their homes. These clearances aroused bitter resentment, especially as many of the flock masters and their shepherds were Lowlanders. Extravagant expectations of profits from wool led to a great increase in rents which the ordinary farmer could not pay. The general effect on Highland economy was summed up in the prophecy—<gai>cuiridh a’ chaora mhaol an crann air an fharadh.<eng>

METRE

The stanzas might be written in one long line, having four stresses and internal rhyme. The stanzas rhyme on ao throughout the poem. As usual in ‘waulking’ songs, the chorus is repeated after each verse.

5048. According to tradition, King Robert the Bruce, after his defeat by the MacDougalls at Dail Rìgh, found shelter in a goat-herd’s hut in Strath Fillan. His gratitude for the kindly service rendered to him there was expressed in the decree that ordained free pasture for goats in that district. (See F, p. 508.)

SONG TO THE INVER

Inbhir (estuary or confluence) is common in place-names. The particular reference here is to Inver at the mouth of the River Kinglass (Conghlais) which enters Loch Etive on the south side, some five miles north-east of Bonawe. Glas (often ‘glass’ in place-names) signifies a stream. (See CPNS, p. 457.)

The poet did not get the traditional Highland welcome on the occasion of his visit to Inver, and his disgust with the house and its occupants found expression in this song. The circumstances remind us of the lampoon on Anne of Cromarty.

The song may have been composed while the poet lived at Dalness, though it was not included in the first two editions.

5167. C, air a gartalan; D, gart-ghlana.

5170. Up to 1806, when slavery was abolished, industry in Barbados depended on slave labour. Negroes were brought from Africa; Cromwell deported many Irishmen to the island; some people who were involved in Monmouth’s rebellion were banished to Barbados.

A number of Highlanders who were involved in the ‘Forty-five’ surrendered under a promise of protection. Despite this promise some of those men were transported, without trial, to Barbados and the Carolinas. (Inv. G.S. Tr., XXIX, p. 17)

This island was an important centre during the Dutch and French wars. In 1761, before the close of the Seven Years’ War, the Black Watch embarked for Barbados, and the name would be quite familiar to the poet.


FIRST TRADE

This neat little poem, based on the story of the Garden of Eden (Gen. 3), is full of humorous touches. The poet’s compliment to the tailoring craft makes some amends for his violent attack on the tailor.

The following is the last stanza of a long poem by Farquhar MacDonell entitled Ceud Oidheirp an Duine—A Shonas agus a Thuiteam:

<gai>B’e ’n ceud obair ’n uair chaill iad gach gràs,
Air dhoibh fhaicinn mar bhà iad gun dreach,
Ionnsuidh ghraid air an nochd ’us an nàir’,
Air an craicionn ’s an cnàimhean a’ chleith,
Rinn iad ap’rain a dhuilleach nan craobh,
’S b’ iad an snàthadan caol bhioran phreas,
’S ann ’s an tàillearachd ’chuir iad an làmh,
’S gur i Màthair gach ceàirde gu beachd.<eng>

(Inv. G.S. Tr., VII, p. 64)


VERSES ON A SWORD

The poem was composed on the occasion of Macintyre’s visit to Taymouth in March 1793. Recruiting for the Breadalbane Fencible regiment was in progress, and the presentation of the sword may have marked his own enrolment in the first battalion. The description of the sword might suggest that he was contrasting it with that other blade he carried as a youthful militiaman some forty-seven years before.

EARL OF BREADALBANE

The fourth Earl succeeded to the titles and estates of Breadalbane in 1782. He was descended from Colin Campbell of Mochaster, second son of Sir Robert Campbell, ninth Laird of Glenorchy. (See Lament for the Earl, 4729, note.) In 1831, when William IV was crowned, the fourth Earl was created Earl of Ormelie and Marquis of Breadalbane in the peerage of the United Kingdom.

When war with the French Republic was declared in 1793 the Earl offered to raise a Fencible regiment for service in Scotland. He himself was appointed commanding officer, with power to nominate the officers of the regiment. Recruiting under the direction of the Earl’s cousin, James Campbell of Glen Falloch, was so successful that two battalions were raised in March and April 1794, although from 8th April the age of enlistment was lowered from 45 to 36 years.

The regiment was recruited largely from the Earl’s estates in Perthshire and Argyll, the Breadalbane men being drafted to

[TD 538]

the first battalion. The poet served with this battalion, as his discharge clearly shows.

In 1794 a third battalion of the Breadalbane Fencible Regiment was raised, and its range of service was extended to Ireland. This battalion was stationed in Ireland until it was disbanded in 1802.

See Famed Breadalbane, p. 196, 203; Sketches of the Highlanders, II, p. 388.

METRE

The first half of the stanza may be written (as it is in CD) in two long lines, each having four stresses, internal rhyme and dactylic end-rhyme. The second half of the stanza consists of three two-stressed phrases, with trochaic end-rhyme, repeated initially in the fourth line, which repeats the dactylic end-rhyme of the first half.

E introduces Air fonn:

Gum b’ eil mo ghràdh air an tàilear acuineach.

[TD 539]

BREADALBANE REGIMENT

See note on the preceding poem; also F, p. 508.

The first battalion of the regiment was embodied in March 1793; it was stationed at Aberdeen in March 1794, at Glasgow in August 1794, at Falkirk in March 1795, at Ayr in March 1796, at Banff in September 1796, at Aberdeen in April 1798, and at Fort George in April 1799, when the battalion was disbanded.

GAZETTE

The first and second battalions of the Breadalbane Fencible regiment were raised for service in Scotland only. This limitation so restricted their usefulness that it was decided in 1798 to disband these two battalions. The official intimation of this decision was heard by the poet at Peterhead, when the first battalion was stationed at Aberdeen. The news of demobilisation was not welcome to him: he had enjoyed the comradeship and leisure of military service, and the prospect of a return to civil life did not appeal to him.

The first battalion was disbanded at Fort George, in April 1799. The poet’s discharge bears the date 18th April, 1799.

METRE

The stanza is a couplet with trochaic end-rhyme on à throughout the poem.

[TD 540]

5452. màdar: a yellow-flowered plant (rubia). Its roots yield a red dye (Eng. madder).

SONG OF THE CAMP

The second battalion of the Breadalbane Fencible regiment was stationed at various places in Scotland: Dumfries (December 1793); Dundee (July 1794); Banff (November 1794); Aberdeen (June 1795 to July 1796); Kirkcaldy (March 1798); Glasgow (June 1798); Ayr (June 1798). At Ayr 260 men volunteered for service in Ireland and sailed from Port Patrick in September 1798; the others (290) were discharged in Irvine and Beith on 18th April, 1799.

See F, p. 508; Famed Breadalbane, p. 203.

The poem suggests that the first battalion had also been invited to enrol for service in Ireland, but there is no evidence that the poet was successful in his endeavour to induce men to volunteer. He himself would have welcomed an extension of service.

Alexander Leith served in various regiments from 1772 to 1783; he inherited his estate from his relative, Andrew Hay of Rannes, and adopted the name Leith Hay; he was Colonel of Hay’s Regiment in 1794 and became Major-General in 1796 (F, p. 510).

METRE

The stanza might be written in four long lines, each bearing four stresses and having end-rhyme throughout the stanza; the second and third stressed vowels correspond to each other, and in some cases to the first stressed vowel.

For refrain the last two lines are repeated in each stanza, as shown in the first.

FINAL FAREWELL TO THE BENS

A footnote in C fixes the date of the poet’s visit to his beloved mountain as 19th September, 1802. The song was composed next day, reminding us of Wordsworth’s “emotion recollected in tranquillity.” The poem is not a description of mountain scenery, but a series of reminiscences, touched by the glamour of the former days and old associations. Macintyre, however, never remained long in sentimental mood: in the midst of regrets for departed joys he derives consolation from the regular income ensured by his service in the Town Guard of Edinburgh. We may well doubt the story that he was so much overcome by emotion as to require his brother’s assistance to finish the poem. (See F, P. 511.)

The circumstances of its composition, its theme of halcyon days, its simple phrasing and fine tune have combined to make Cead deireannach nam beann one of the best-known songs in Gaelic.

TITLE

àit is the whole region; iad recalls the various features mentioned.

Some modern copies of this song have changed àit to ait. If this implies that the poet had been glad to leave Glen Orchy for Edinburgh, the reading would be b’ ait a leig.

The last quatrain has a double rhyme throughout, the first on à and the second on ì.


EPITAPH

The third edition ends with this didactic poem, just as the second edition finishes with Comh-dhùnadh. In these verses, possibly the last he composed, as they seem to have been hurriedly added at the end of C, the aged bard feels that he has run his course on earth, and imagines himself speaking from the tomb.

A note to this poem in E (1848) suggests that the 1st, 4th and 7th stanzas would make a suitable inscription for the poet’s tombstone. In 1855 a monument was erected over the poet’s grave in Greyfriars Churchyard, Edinburgh, and the first stanza of the poem is inscribed on one side.

TITLE

No title in C, but a footnote, “The Author’s Epitaph, by himself.”

The last stanza of this poem recalls the last lines written by Sir Walter Raleigh:

Even such is time that takes in trust
Our youth, our joys, our all we have,
And pays us with but age and dust;
Who, in the dark and silent grave,
When we have wandered all our ways,
Shuts up the story of our days;
But from this earth, this grave, this dust,
The Lord shall raise me up, I trust.


SONG TO JOHN WILKES

In the third edition a list of subscribers is inserted after the Epitaph, and after the list comes the poem by John Macintyre, Oban:

Rann a rinneadh le Iain Macantsaoir ’san Oban.

This kindly, humorous tribute to Donnchadh Bàn by a contemporary shows such familiarity with the poet’s work that the eighth stanza would warrant the inclusion of Oran Iain Faochag in the text of Macintyre’s poems.

[TD 545]

John Macintyre’s poem had nine stanzas of which Nos. 2, 3, 7 and 8 are quoted below.

<gai>Nam marbhadh iad thu air do thurus,
Bu mhuladach Baile Dhùn Eideann
Mur rachadh tu rithisd dachaigh
A thoirt eachdraidh air na féidh dhaibh;
A’ bhliadhna thàinig an t-aodach tartain,
’S cead nam breacan chur an fhéile,
’S tusa rinn Rann na Pìoba,
’S mhol thu innleachdan a’ Ghreumaich.

Mhol thu gu sònraichte Righ Deòrsa
Air son an t-seòil a rinn e féin duinn:
Thug e am fearann do na fògraich
A chuir an gòraich’ as a chéile;
Dh’ innis thu gach math a rinn iad,
Mhol thu saighdearan gach treud dhiubh; [E, treubh]
Mur deachaidh tu tuille ’s fada
Cha d’ fhàg thu aon dad an déislaimh.

Mhol thu air thoiseach an Rìgh,
’S thug thu onoir phrìseil dhà-san;
Mhol thu daoin’-uailse na rìoghachd,
Gun dìochuimhn air an luchd-àitich;
Mhol thu na féidh is na frìthean,
’S gach nì tha cinntinn am fàsach;
Dhèanadh tu moladh no dìteadh,
Ard no ìosal, mar a b’ àill leat.

A h-uile bàrd a bha riut mìomhail,
Chan ’eil dìth mar fhuair iad pàigheadh:
Mhill thu ’n cliù air Uisdean pìobair’
A bha strìth riut an Cinn t-Sàile;
Teisneas Anna rinn thu sgaoileadh
Feadh an t-saoghail air dhroch càradh;
’S olc a dhiomoil thu Iain Faochag, [E, ’S math]
’S cha b’ ann faoin a dh’ aoir thu ’n tàillear.<eng>

But John Macintyre’s reference is not the only evidence we have that Donnchadh Bàn was the author of this satire. Oran Iain Faochag le D. Macantsaoir was published in Edinburgh in 1768 as a pamphlet of four pages. The pamphlet was sewn into the second edition (B, 1790) after the list of subscribers, at least in some copies. The type is smaller than the type used in B, but the pages are of the same size as the page of B. The poem was omitted from C, but it is included in the text of D and E, and given as an appendix in F.

[TD 546]

John Wilkes was born in London in 1727. The son of a wealthy brewer who owned much property, he received a good education privately and in the University of Leyden. He was elected M.P. for Aylesburgh in 1759, and proved to be a formidable and unscrupulous opponent of Grenville and the Earl of Bute. After being expelled in 1764 from the House of Commons for seditious libel, he fled to France and was outlawed.

In 1768 Wilkes was returned as Member of Parliament for Middlesex, but was expelled and imprisoned for the libel uttered in 1763 in his paper, North Briton. He was re-elected three times but was refused permission to take his seat in the Commons. In 1769 he was elected an alderman, and became Lord Mayor of London in 1772.

John Wilkes received great popular support, and much money was subscribed to defend the cause of “Wilkes and Liberty.” He became the champion of the right of free representation by British constituencies, as well as of the freedom of the press; but he was a man of vicious character, and this is the only side that Macintyre describes. The life and character of John Wilkes afforded so much material for satire that it was unnecessary to descend to the scurrilous and pointless abuse we find in some stanzas of this poem.

The poet would have seen the burning of the effigies of unpopular public men, including that of John Wilkes, on the occasion of the annual festival of drinking the King’s health, when judges, magistrates and leading citizens gathered in the Parliament Hall for a public repast. The city guard were on duty at the Hall and suffered much indignity at the hands of the rabble.

(Wilson’s Memorials, p. 218)

5712. deachamh (i) tenth part, tithe, e.g. Gen. 14, 20; Mal., 3, 8. (ii) decimation, destruction.

<gai>Bha iomadh Gàidheal ’s an deachamh.<eng> (Bàrdachd, 755.)

The following notes from Fr. Allan MacDonald’s MSS. were supplied by Mr. J. L. Campbell:

(1) ugh deachamh, an addled egg;

(2) dol ’san deachamh, disappearing;

(3) cuir ’san deachamh, put out of the way.

(4) “When eggs are set for hatching it is always an odd number that is set. It is said that there is always one egg which fails to be hatched. This egg is said to go into the deachamh.”

With (1) compare ugh deachadh, ugh deachaidh. (Dwelly.)

Cf. Rob Donn, Poems, p. 237:

<gai>Ma théid gràine dheth ’n diuchaidh.<eng>

The meaning is apparently ‘loss’ or ‘wastage.’

Forbes (p. 300), referring to the lark’s nest:

An infertile egg is disposed of somehow, and in olden time people used to say that it was into the deachamh the lark put it, and the lark was regarded as a blessed bird. [This appears to associate the egg with tithe, though the egg might be a worthless offering.]

The following note by W. J. Watson (Celtic Review, V, p. 154) may be relevant:

“In E. Ross (once a stronghold of the Celtic Church) the past generation were in the habit of applying the term iobart colloquially to any unkempt ‘ill-guided’ creature, whether beast or body. Was this a sinister reminiscence of the usual condition of animals presented to the Church?”

Similarly in Perthshire, Rev. C. M. Robertson noted that rinn thu iobairt dhe meant ‘you have made a muddle or a hash of it.’
(Inv. G.S. Tr., XXII, p. 42.)

[TD 548]

The less pious folk who found clerical demands irksome may have devoted inferior specimens of animals, fowls, or eggs to the mixed tithe.

[TD 549]

ELEGY ON A DOG

In the fourth edition (D, 1834), Oran Iain Faochag ends at p. 201, and is followed by five stanzas of John Macintyre’s song from C, with CRIOCH duly printed at the foot of p. 202. But on p. 203, without explanation or reference to source, we find this poem added as a postscript, under the title:

MARBH-RANN,
LE DONNACHADH MACANTSAOIR,
DO CHUTH A CHAIDH TROIMH ’N EIDHE, SA MHAIACH
TARSAING NA BHEUL

Mr Angus Matheson informs the editor that a printed copy of this ‘elegy’ is bound, after John Macintyre’s song, in the copy of the third edition (C, 1804) that belonged to Rev. Joseph Macintyre, D.D., and that after the poet’s name in the title, the following note, presumably in Dr Macintyre’s handwriting, is written: “The Bard of Nature and Ossian of this age, aged 81, March 1805.”

The poem was evidently published as a leaflet before that date. The leaflet version is followed by D, but the tall s in sùil (see 5817) is mistaken by D for f. (The tall s is not found in C or D, but is normally used in B, except at the end of words.)

The authorship may thus be established, but the poem raises other problems. The last line seems to indicate personal interest in a recent incident; but the verse reads like a tale of former days, before guns were used in the chase, when the royal forest of Glen Artney was the scene of great hunts. It may be that the poet is retelling an old story, but whether it refers to the Pàdraig of Beinn Dóbhrain (see 2807) or to some other famous hunter it is impossible to say. The story is told in the title: there is no mention of ice in the text.

The Forest of Glen Artney lies east of Ben Vorlich and south of St Fillans, overlooking Glen Artney to the south-east.

METRE

Rannaigheachd dialtach, (7¹ + 7¹) in 1st and 3rd stanzas;
rannaigheachd bheag mhór (8² + 8²) in 2nd stanza.

TEXT

5816. D, gaodhar; E, gaothar.

E.Ir. gagar; O.N. gagarr; M.Ir. gadar.

5817. D, fùil a’s malladh.

5819. EF, An curaidh.

5820–1: like a ‘run’ in old Gaelic tales.

[TD 550]

5822. D, mhonnadh; E, mhonadh.

5823. EF, tàinig e riamh; E, dhathigh; F, dhathaigh.

5826. D, na’m beist’ duth; EF, biast.

biast-dubh or béist-dubh is a common term for otter; also biast-donn.

Dwelly gives ruadh-bhiast, moor-fowl; but ruadhag (young deer), or madadh ruadh (fox) may be meant.

Cf.
<gai>An coire ’m faigh duin’-uasal
Biast dhubh is ruadh ’n a chàrn.<eng>
(AMD, p. 54)

5830. D, bha’d.

5830. bonn ri bonn: sole to sole, interlocked, e.g. tuiteadar bonn ri bonn, they fell fighting hand to hand. (Dinn.)


ANOTHER POEM ON THE BATTLE OF FALKIRK

The fifth edition (E) adds this poem to the text of D, placing it immediately after the first poem, with an explanatory footnote:

“This song was excluded by the Author from three editions of his work printed for himself, because it was a Jacobite piece and offensive to the Campbells who always were his best patrons. …”

“For the set here given we had recourse partly to oral recitation and partly to Turner’s Collection of Gaelic Songs. …”

Turner (1813), p. 117, names Donnchadh Bàn as the author, but this ascription may be founded on popular association. While the Turner version is obviously defective (e.g. 5901–2, 5908–9), variant readings show that “oral recitation” made little contribution to the version in E.

After the first six stanzas the poem has no relevance to the title. Praise of the Camerons and MacDonalds, to the exclusion of other clans that fought on the moors of Falkirk and Drumossie, is a feature of the poem; and its subject is the aftermath of Culloden rather than the Battle of Falkirk. The complaint of unfair conditions and tactics, the pathetic reliance on foreign aid, and the threat of a future rising remind us of other Jacobite poems.

It is not an impressive production, and there is no apparent reason why Macintyre should have composed it, nor is it easy to specify a period when he could have done so. He had already described the rout of the Hanoverians at Falkirk with a full meed of praise for Clan Donald, and he had discussed wittily and

[TD 551]

forcefully the Act for the Pacification of the Highlands. There is nothing new in this poem except two names. Calum mac Phàraig is of no importance, and Iain mac Ruairidh, though apparently influential, is a mysterious personage. In the footnote quoted above, E suggests he was Prince Charles, but there is no need for a code word here, when the Prince is referred to explicitly in the last six stanzas. The best-known bearer of this name was Iain Breac, Chief of the MacLeods of Skye (d. 1693), whose praises were sung by Roderick Morrison. The latter’s son, the blind poet and piper (1666–1754), was also referred to sometimes as Iain mac Ruairidh.

It will be noticed that several stanzas of the poem begin by repeating a part of the last couplet of the preceding stanza. This linking of stanzas is not a feature of Macintyre’s poetry, but is not uncommon in northern verse.

If Macintyre was the author, the cuideachd (5834) would appear to be the Argyll Militia; but the first stanza is hardly explained by normal disbandment. The poem, indeed, reads like an old man’s plaint, expressive of personal disappointment or thwarted political ambition. Macintyre had no political aspirations, and it is difficult to believe that he would compose this medley of Jacobite verses in his old age, using metre, matter and title already employed by himself, and reminding his patrons, the Campbells, of the debacle at Inverlochy.

TITLE

As given in Turner (1813), p. 117:

Oran air blar na h-Eaglaise-brice, am bliadhna 1745.
Le Donnacha Bàn Mac an t-Saoir.
Fonn—Alasdair dubh Ghlinne Garaidh
(The battle was fought on 17th January, 1746.)

Note. T indicates reading in Turner’s Collection.


METRE AND TUNE

See first poem.

The song is still popular in the oral tradition of Uist and Barra.

VERSES RECITED BY A MAIDEN FOR HER LOVER

This curiosity first appeared in B.

The text shows that the title is quite inappropriate. Here we have, not verses sung or recited by a maiden, but a love charm, with detailed instructions regarding ingredients and application. If we accept the assurance given by the editors of B, C, D that all the poems in these editions were composed by Macintyre, then our poet must have invented this charm and be reckoned “a dealer in magic and spells.” We can only surmise that these lines were known to the poet and included by his first editor as a piece of folk-lore. They were copied in CDEF, without comment, except a note by Dr Calder (F, p. 507):

“Versions of this [the first] quatrain, which is probably an old Love-charm, will be found in Carmina Gadelica, II, 38, 40, 41.”

The two versions referred to in this note extend to the whole poem. The first (X), p. 38, is headed EOLAS GRADHAICH; the second (Y), p. 40, has the title EOLAS GRADHAIDH. The latter differs considerably from the other versions.

J. G. Campbell discussed this ‘charm’ in his book Witchcraft and Second Sight in the Scottish Highlands, p. 82, and noted that the ‘charm’ is ascribed by some to Duncan Macintyre, and by others

[TD 554]

“with more probability to Blind Allan, the Glengarry bard,” who was supposed to eke out a livelihood by the practice of such charms. There is no attempt to explain how Allan MacDougall’s verses came to be included in Macintyre’s second edition without reference or acknowledgment.

TITLE

BC, ga leannan; DEF, d’ a.

METRE

BCDE: 20 lines, no stanzas; F: one quatrain, and one stanza of 16 lines. X: five quatrains, two irregular in metre. Y: six quatrains.

Lines are mostly of the form 6¹ or 6², and even lines have end-rhyme.

TEXT

5989. BCD, lic chomhnairt; E, lic chòmhnairt; F, lic còmhnairt; X, leac comhnard pleatach; Y, lic chomhnard chladaich.

B, phlattaich; CDEF, phlataich.

5990. BCDEF, beannachd pobuill (“folk’s boon,” F). This does not suit tog, loisg. X, puball beannach (“pinnacled canopy; butter-bur?”); Y, beannach pubaill (“pointed canopy; butter-bur?”).

5991. BCDEFXY, currachd. F, “priest’s hood”; X, “the biretta of a priest; foxglove?”; Y, “the cap of a priest; foxglove?”

5992. X, air do; air a, ar a, or a are common in speech for air do. cf. “Ar a bhonnagan a ghaoil.” (Hebridean song.)

5993. BCDEF, agus sluasaid; XY, ann an sluasaid.

5996. XY, cnamhan. “The lucky bones are the joint of the big toe of the right foot, and the nail-joints of the left foot of an old man.” (Carmina Gadelica II, p. 39.)

A note on p. 372 suggests a possible reference here to lus an t-seann duine (southernwood), but prefers the literal meaning.

It would thus appear that all the ingredients for the recipe might be plants, which could be obtained much more easily than the items suggested by a literal interpretation. It would be necessary to read o uaigh for a uaigh.

[TD 555]

NOTE ON THE MAPS OF GLEN ETIVE AND BEN DOBHRAIN

(Numbers refer to lines of text. OS = Ordnance Survey.)

Map A (Glen Etive district) shows most of the localities referred to in Song to a Ewe (1860) and some of the place-names in Song to the Gun (3347–70). The ‘short cut’ by Coire Chaolain (3364) points to Claisgour, not Dalness, as the poet’s home at the time.

Map B covers the region of Ben Dóbhrain and the upper part of Glen Lochay, including Misty Corrie. Places mentioned in the description of the ben and the corrie, and in Song to the Gun (except 3347–70), are found in this area.

A map of the “Forrest of Mamlorne faithfully survey’d conform to an Act and Warrand of the Rt. Honble the Lords of Council and Session” by David Dowie in 1735 was lent to the editor by the late Rev. W. A. Gillies, D.D., of Kenmore, and was reproduced in Sàr Orain.

The following list* gives names in English form as recorded by Dowie:

“Water of Deige” (Uisge Srath na Dìge, 2518) is Allt Badamhaim in Map B, a tributary of the Lochay.

“Burn of Finglen Beg” (Allt Fionna Ghlinne in Map B, 2415) flows north into Loch Lyon.

“Burn of Larigm Padzie” is Allt Learg Macbheattie in Map B, and Allt Mhic Bhàidein in OS (cf. 2310). It is a tributary of “Water of Longean” (Abhainn Ghlas in Map B) which flows into the west end of Loch Lyon.

“Burn of Aldeheach” (All tCeothach in Map B; Allt Cheathaich in OS) is shown in Dowie’s map flowing through “Corry Altrum” into “Water of Lochay.” “Corry Alltrum” was thus a local or an earlier name for Coire a’ Cheathaich. The pass into the corrie from the west is described as “Passage to and from the Nursery.” The term “Nursery” could be applied to many corries in a deer forest (cf. 3356), and the “Corry Altrum” of Dowie’s map need not be the corrie of 2880.

“Tuilim” (Tuilm, 2415), indexed by Dowie as “a little rock,” stands north of the “Passage.”

“Phree Corriean” near the “Passage” cannot be the Frith-Choirean of 3158.


* To this list we have to add Ruadh Aisridh (2302), a small burn on the west side of Coire a’ Cheathaich.

[TD 556]

“Malltennell” (Meall Tionail in Map B), situated south of the west end of Loch Lyon, is not the Meall Teanail of 3129.

“Craigmore” (Creag Mhór) in Map B, overlooking “Corry Altrum,” is the Creag Mhór of 2326, but not necessarily the Creag Mhór of 3071. There is a hill of this name east of Achallader, but 3071 may refer to some craggy height within the bounds of Ben Dóbhrain.

The localities known to be situated within these bounds may be noted in course of a circular tour of the ben, as detailed in Sàr Orain, pp. 224–25. Following the railway or the old road south from Bridge of Orchy, we skirt the western flank lethtaobh na Leacainn (3156), above which stands Frith-Choirean (3158); then we pass Doire Chrò (3078) about a mile north of Auch. Fuaran Mór (3082) is marked above Doire Chrò (3078), and An t-Sròin (3079) overlooks Auch.

Turning north through Auch Glen and following the old track by Allt Chonghlais (River Auch) we note three burns (not named in Map B) flowing down the south-eastern slope of the ben. At the head of the first is Coire Fhraoich (3178); the second rises in Coire a’ Chruiteir* (2844)—Coire Chruitein in OS; and the third, draining the southern face of Meall Teanail (3129)—Meall Tionail in Map B—is Allt na h-annaid (3130). The chapel (annaid) and the burial ground were situated in the northern angle of the confluence of this tributary with the Auch. Craobh na h-Ainnis (3106), according to tradition, stood on the opposite bank of the Auch at this point. (See F, pp. 511, 531.)

Less than a mile north of Annaid we come to the junction of another tributary, Allt a’ Chùirn, which flows from the county boundary, in the valley between Beinn a’ Chùirn and Beinn nam Fuaran (3338)—Beinn Fhuaran in B. At the junction we find Sìthean Mór and Sìthean Beag, with Ais an t-Sìthein (2885) between the streams. Here stands the cottage occupied until recently by one of the shepherds of Auch farm. West of Ais an t-Sìthein we note Creag Sheileach (3117, 3268) on the eastern face of Meall Teanail (3129)—Meall Tionail in B.

The northern part of Allt Chonglais, above Allt Coire a’ Ghabhalaich, is named Allt an Lòin; it flows from Lòn na Caillich on the county boundary north-west of Beinn a’ Chùirn. Connlon (3113) is


* The name Coire a’ Chruiteir has been rather fancifully explained by reference to the twanging sounds made by certain winds in the corrie; but the OS form of the name is modern and may have been suggested by a name like Gleann Cruitean, Oban.

[TD 557]

probably to be located in this area. Làrach na Féinne (3116) lies in Gleann Cailliche, east of Beinn Ach-Chaladair (3112); and Lurgainn na Laoidhre (3114) is a shank-like strip of land between Allt an Lòin and Allt Coire a’ Ghabhalaich at their confluence (OS Lurg Luibheach).

The headwaters of the latter stream converge at a point half a mile south of Coire Réidh (3112), situated on the southern face of Beinn an Dothaidh; Coire Daingean (3107) also lies on this ben, at the head of Allt Coire Ach-Chaladair, and higher up the ben is Coire Rainich (3110). Am Bealach (3111) may be the pass between Beinn an Dothaidh and Beinn Ach-Chaladair.
